\section{Corrected Residual Quartic Closure: Exact Four-Class Upgrade}

\section{Current stage}

We freeze the operational backend and the corrected local branch as
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
(\mu^2,\lambda,\gamma)=(0.26,-0.43,1.62),
\qquad
\delta^{\mathrm{final}}=(0.0096,-0.0288,0.0096).
}
\end{equation}

The correct reduced object is the projected residual map
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
G(\delta)
=
\begin{pmatrix}
G_A(\delta)\\
G_E(\delta)\\
G_O(\delta)
\end{pmatrix},
\qquad
G_X(\delta):=
\Re\langle \Phi_X,R[\Psi(\delta)]\rangle_\Omega,
\quad
X\in\{A,E,O\},
}
\end{equation}
not a naive reduced free-energy critical point.

The corrected branch diagnostics are
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
\|G(\delta^{\mathrm{final}})\|
\approx
0.03791852545979427,
\qquad
\texttt{rel\_residual}(\delta^{\mathrm{final}})
\approx
0.036459908397927156,
}
\end{equation}
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
\texttt{shell\_fraction}(\delta^{\mathrm{final}})
\approx
0.9156153162713265,
\qquad
\texttt{orbit\_balance}(\delta^{\mathrm{final}})
\approx
0.8860846137625883.
}
\end{equation}

\section{Corrected projected quartic data}

The residual-based extractor gives the quartic class means
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
U_d=2493.1087739398513,
\quad
U_{\mathrm{opp}}=14874.171297437068,
\quad
U_\perp=9027.17290917827,
\quad
U_{\mathrm{obl}}=2244.8894311890813.
}
\end{equation}

If one compresses these numbers into the old three-parameter shell kernel
\begin{equation}
U_{\alpha\beta}^{\mathrm{simple}}
=
u\,\delta_{\alpha\beta}
+
v
+
\kappa(\hat k_\alpha\!\cdot\!\hat k_\beta)^2,
\end{equation}
then one obtains
\begin{equation}
u=-12381.062523497216,
\qquad
v=9027.17290917827,
\qquad
\kappa=-27129.133911956756,
\end{equation}
but the corresponding exact defect is
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
\Delta_{\mathrm{ker}}=32976.132300215555.
}
\end{equation}

Hence the \((u,v,\kappa)\) truncation fails strongly even on the corrected branch.

\section{Why the three-parameter truncation fails mathematically}

For a BCC shell, ordered shell pairs \((\alpha,\beta)\) fall into exactly four classes:
\begin{equation}
\alpha=\beta,
\qquad
\hat k_\beta=-\hat k_\alpha,
\qquad
\hat k_\alpha\cdot\hat k_\beta=0,
\qquad
|\hat k_\alpha\cdot\hat k_\beta|=\frac12.
\end{equation}

For the old ansatz
\begin{equation}
U_{\alpha\beta}^{\mathrm{simple}}
=
u\,\delta_{\alpha\beta}
+
v
+
\kappa(\hat k_\alpha\!\cdot\!\hat k_\beta)^2,
\end{equation}
the four class values are forced to satisfy
\begin{equation}
U_d=u+v+\kappa,
\qquad
U_{\mathrm{opp}}=v+\kappa,
\qquad
U_\perp=v,
\qquad
U_{\mathrm{obl}}=v+\frac{\kappa}{4}.
\end{equation}

Therefore the old truncation imposes the exact algebraic constraint
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
4U_{\mathrm{obl}}-3U_\perp-U_{\mathrm{opp}}=0.
}
\end{equation}

Equivalently,
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
\Delta_{\mathrm{ker}}
:=
4U_{\mathrm{obl}}-3U_\perp-U_{\mathrm{opp}}
}
\end{equation}
must vanish identically on the old three-parameter submanifold.

Using the corrected projected data,
\begin{align}
4U_{\mathrm{obl}}
&=
8979.5577247563252,
\\
3U_\perp
&=
27081.51872753481,
\\
4U_{\mathrm{obl}}-3U_\perp-U_{\mathrm{opp}}
&=
-32976.132300215555.
\end{align}
Thus
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
|\Delta_{\mathrm{ker}}|=32976.132300215555\gg 0,
}
\end{equation}
so the simple kernel is not approximately wrong but structurally impossible.

\section{Minimal exact enlargement: four-class shell kernel}

Let
\begin{equation}
P^{(d)}_{\alpha\beta}:=\mathbf 1_{\{\alpha=\beta\}},
\qquad
P^{(\mathrm{opp})}_{\alpha\beta}:=\mathbf 1_{\{\hat k_\beta=-\hat k_\alpha\}},
\end{equation}
\begin{equation}
P^{(\perp)}_{\alpha\beta}:=\mathbf 1_{\{\hat k_\alpha\cdot\hat k_\beta=0\}},
\qquad
P^{(\mathrm{obl})}_{\alpha\beta}:=\mathbf 1_{\{|\hat k_\alpha\cdot\hat k_\beta|=1/2\}}.
\end{equation}
Then
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
P^{(d)}+P^{(\mathrm{opp})}+P^{(\perp)}+P^{(\mathrm{obl})}=\mathbf 1
}
\end{equation}
on ordered shell-pair space.

The minimal exact enlarged quartic shell kernel is therefore
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
U_{\alpha\beta}^{(4c)}
=
u_d\,P^{(d)}_{\alpha\beta}
+
u_{\mathrm{opp}}\,P^{(\mathrm{opp})}_{\alpha\beta}
+
u_\perp\,P^{(\perp)}_{\alpha\beta}
+
u_{\mathrm{obl}}\,P^{(\mathrm{obl})}_{\alpha\beta}.
}
\end{equation}

Equivalently,
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
U_{\alpha\beta}^{(4c)}
=
\begin{cases}
u_d, & \alpha=\beta,\\[1mm]
u_{\mathrm{opp}}, & \hat k_\beta=-\hat k_\alpha,\\[1mm]
u_\perp, & \hat k_\alpha\cdot\hat k_\beta=0,\\[1mm]
u_{\mathrm{obl}}, & |\hat k_\alpha\cdot\hat k_\beta|=\frac12.
\end{cases}
}
\end{equation}

This is the smallest exact shell kernel that resolves all four BCC pair classes independently.

\section{Direct coefficient identification on the corrected branch}

Because the four-class kernel resolves the classes exactly, its coefficients are not fitted indirectly but read off directly:
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
u_d=U_d,
\qquad
u_{\mathrm{opp}}=U_{\mathrm{opp}},
\qquad
u_\perp=U_\perp,
\qquad
u_{\mathrm{obl}}=U_{\mathrm{obl}}.
}
\end{equation}

Hence on the corrected branch,
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
u_d=2493.1087739398513,
\quad
u_{\mathrm{opp}}=14874.171297437068,
\quad
u_\perp=9027.17290917827,
\quad
u_{\mathrm{obl}}=2244.8894311890813.
}
\end{equation}

So the four-class upgrade is not a heuristic ansatz.
It is the exact minimal interpolation of the already extracted corrected quartic data.

\section{Reduced quartic form on the orbit-amplitude sector}

Let the shell amplitudes be reduced to three orbit sectors
\[
A,\qquad E,\qquad O,
\]
with real amplitudes
\[
a_A,\qquad a_E,\qquad a_O,
\]
and define
\[
r_A=a_A^2,
\qquad
r_E=a_E^2,
\qquad
r_O=a_O^2.
\]

The quartic shell contribution is
\begin{equation}
F_4^{\mathrm{red}}
=
\frac14
\sum_{\alpha,\beta\in S_*}
U_{\alpha\beta}^{(4c)}
|\psi_\alpha|^2|\psi_\beta|^2.
\end{equation}

After orbit reduction, it becomes
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
F_4^{\mathrm{red}}(r_A,r_E,r_O)
=
\frac14
\sum_{X,Y\in\{A,E,O\}}
M_{XY}\,r_X r_Y,
}
\end{equation}
with
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
M_{XY}
=
N^{(d)}_{XY}u_d
+
N^{(\mathrm{opp})}_{XY}u_{\mathrm{opp}}
+
N^{(\perp)}_{XY}u_\perp
+
N^{(\mathrm{obl})}_{XY}u_{\mathrm{obl}}.
}
\end{equation}

Here
\[
N^{(c)}_{XY}
\]
is the purely combinatorial number of ordered shell pairs with first index in orbit \(X\), second index in orbit \(Y\), and pair-class \(c\).

Thus the enlarged quartic closure problem is reduced to a finite combinatorial computation:
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
\{N^{(d)}_{XY},N^{(\mathrm{opp})}_{XY},N^{(\perp)}_{XY},N^{(\mathrm{obl})}_{XY}\}
\Longrightarrow
M_{XY}.
}
\end{equation}

\section{Residual-level upgrade}

Because the correct reduced object is the residual map, the quartic contribution must enter through
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
G_X^{(4)}(a_A,a_E,a_O)
=
\frac{\partial F_4^{\mathrm{red}}}{\partial a_X},
\qquad
X\in\{A,E,O\}.
}
\end{equation}

Using \(r_X=a_X^2\), this gives
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
G_X^{(4)}
=
a_X\sum_{Y\in\{A,E,O\}} M_{XY}r_Y.
}
\end{equation}

Therefore the corrected reduced residual system must be upgraded to
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
G_X(a_A,a_E,a_O)
=
G_X^{(2)}
+
a_X\sum_Y M_{XY}r_Y
+\cdots,
\qquad
X\in\{A,E,O\}.
}
\end{equation}

\section{Minimal theorem-level conclusion}

\begin{theorem}
Let the BCC shell quartic data be reduced to the four class means
\[
(U_d,U_{\mathrm{opp}},U_\perp,U_{\mathrm{obl}}).
\]
Then:

\begin{enumerate}
\item the simple kernel \(u\delta_{\alpha\beta}+v+\kappa(\hat k_\alpha\cdot\hat k_\beta)^2\) is exact if and only if
\[
4U_{\mathrm{obl}}-3U_\perp-U_{\mathrm{opp}}=0;
\]

\item if this condition fails, the minimal exact enlargement is the four-class kernel
\[
U_{\alpha\beta}^{(4c)}
=
u_dP^{(d)}_{\alpha\beta}
+
u_{\mathrm{opp}}P^{(\mathrm{opp})}_{\alpha\beta}
+
u_\perp P^{(\perp)}_{\alpha\beta}
+
u_{\mathrm{obl}}P^{(\mathrm{obl})}_{\alpha\beta};
\]

\item the resulting orbit-reduced quartic form is determined by the finite matrix
\[
M_{XY}
=
N^{(d)}_{XY}u_d
+
N^{(\mathrm{opp})}_{XY}u_{\mathrm{opp}}
+
N^{(\perp)}_{XY}u_\perp
+
N^{(\mathrm{obl})}_{XY}u_{\mathrm{obl}},
\]
and the quartic residual contribution is
\[
G_X^{(4)}=a_X\sum_Y M_{XY}r_Y.
\]
\end{enumerate}
\end{theorem}

\begin{proof}
Part (1) follows from the explicit class values of the three-parameter kernel.
Part (2) follows because the BCC shell has exactly four pair classes, so any exact pair-class-resolved kernel must have at least four independent coefficients.
Part (3) is the orbit reduction of the shell quartic form followed by differentiation with respect to \(a_X\).
\end{proof}

\section{Sharp next task}

The next nonredundant executable step is now uniquely fixed:
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
(u,v,\kappa)
\longrightarrow
(u_d,u_{\mathrm{opp}},u_\perp,u_{\mathrm{obl}})
\longrightarrow
M_{XY}
\longrightarrow
G_X^{(4)}.
}
\end{equation}

If the residual misfit remains large after this step, then the next obstruction is no longer pair-class compression.
It is genuine higher shell-tensor structure beyond four pair classes.

are frozen.

The old three-parameter shell kernel

\section{Exact Coarse-Orbit Combinatorics of the Corrected Four-Class Quartic Kernel}

\section{Short explanation}

At the current corrected residual stage, the next finite task is to compute the
orbit-count matrices
\[
N^{(d)}_{XY},\qquad
N^{(\mathrm{opp})}_{XY},\qquad
N^{(\perp)}_{XY},\qquad
N^{(\mathrm{obl})}_{XY},
\qquad
X,Y\in\{A,E,O\},
\]
for the coarse orbit split of the commensurate BCC shell.

This part can now be closed exactly.

The key result is that, for the coarse orbit decomposition
\[
|A|=2,\qquad |E|=2,\qquad |O|=8,
\]
the ordered-pair class counts are completely explicit.
As a consequence, the four-class reduced quartic matrix is explicit as well.

A second important consequence is structural:

\[
\boxed{
\text{the four-class pair-class closure is automatically }A\leftrightarrow E\text{ symmetric at coarse-orbit level.}
}
\]

Therefore any \(A/E\) asymmetry in the corrected residual system must come from the quadratic sector
\[
\sigma_A\neq \sigma_E,
\]
or from a refinement beyond coarse pair-class data, e.g. sign-resolved suborbit quartics.

\section{1. Coarse orbit split of the selected BCC shell}

Choose the shell axis and tangent frame
\begin{equation}
\hat n=\frac{(1,1,0)}{\sqrt2},
\qquad
e_1=\frac{(1,-1,0)}{\sqrt2},
\qquad
e_2=(0,0,1).
\end{equation}

Then the 12 first-shell BCC directions split as follows:

\begin{equation}
A=\left\{
\frac{(1,1,0)}{\sqrt2},
\frac{(-1,-1,0)}{\sqrt2}
\right\},
\qquad
|A|=2,
\end{equation}

\begin{equation}
E=\left\{
\frac{(1,-1,0)}{\sqrt2},
\frac{(-1,1,0)}{\sqrt2}
\right\},
\qquad
|E|=2,
\end{equation}

\begin{equation}
O=
\left\{
\frac{(\pm1,0,\pm1)}{\sqrt2},
\frac{(0,\pm1,\pm1)}{\sqrt2}
\right\},
\qquad
|O|=8.
\end{equation}

Thus
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
n_A=2,\qquad n_E=2,\qquad n_O=8.
}
\end{equation}

\section{2. Ordered-pair class-count matrices}

We count ordered pairs \((\alpha,\beta)\) with first index in orbit \(X\), second index in orbit \(Y\),
and class
\[
d,\qquad \mathrm{opp},\qquad \perp,\qquad \mathrm{obl}.
\]

\subsection{Diagonal counts}

Diagonal pairs occur only when \(\alpha=\beta\).
Hence
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
N^{(d)}
=
\begin{pmatrix}
2 & 0 & 0\\
0 & 2 & 0\\
0 & 0 & 8
\end{pmatrix}.
}
\end{equation}

\subsection{Opposite counts}

Each shell direction has exactly one opposite partner in the same coarse orbit.
Therefore
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
N^{(\mathrm{opp})}
=
\begin{pmatrix}
2 & 0 & 0\\
0 & 2 & 0\\
0 & 0 & 8
\end{pmatrix}.
}
\end{equation}

\subsection{Perpendicular counts}

The \(A\)-vectors are perpendicular only to the \(E\)-vectors, and the oblique orbit \(O\) contains
16 ordered perpendicular pairs internally. Thus
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
N^{(\perp)}
=
\begin{pmatrix}
0 & 4 & 0\\
4 & 0 & 0\\
0 & 0 & 16
\end{pmatrix}.
}
\end{equation}

\subsection{Oblique counts}

The \(A\)-vectors are oblique to all 8 \(O\)-vectors, likewise for the \(E\)-vectors,
and the \(O\)-orbit contains 32 ordered oblique pairs internally.
Hence
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
N^{(\mathrm{obl})}
=
\begin{pmatrix}
0 & 0 & 16\\
0 & 0 & 16\\
16 & 16 & 32
\end{pmatrix}.
}
\end{equation}

\subsection{Consistency check}

Summing the four class matrices gives the full ordered-pair count matrix:
\begin{equation}
N^{(d)}+N^{(\mathrm{opp})}+N^{(\perp)}+N^{(\mathrm{obl})}
=
\begin{pmatrix}
4 & 4 & 16\\
4 & 4 & 16\\
16 & 16 & 64
\end{pmatrix}
=
\begin{pmatrix}
n_A n_A & n_A n_E & n_A n_O\\
n_E n_A & n_E n_E & n_E n_O\\
n_O n_A & n_O n_E & n_O n_O
\end{pmatrix}.
\end{equation}
So the combinatorics is complete and exact.

\section{3. Raw four-class orbit matrix on the corrected branch}

The corrected class means are
\begin{equation}
u_d=2493.1087739398513,
\quad
u_{\mathrm{opp}}=14874.171297437068,
\quad
u_\perp=9027.17290917827,
\quad
u_{\mathrm{obl}}=2244.8894311890813.
\end{equation}

Under the raw ordered-pair convention,
\begin{equation}
M^{\mathrm{raw}}_{XY}
=
N^{(d)}_{XY}u_d
+
N^{(\mathrm{opp})}_{XY}u_{\mathrm{opp}}
+
N^{(\perp)}_{XY}u_\perp
+
N^{(\mathrm{obl})}_{XY}u_{\mathrm{obl}}.
\end{equation}

Therefore
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
M^{\mathrm{raw}}
=
\begin{pmatrix}
34734.560142753835 & 36108.69163671308 & 35918.23089902530\\
36108.69163671308 & 34734.560142753835 & 35918.23089902530\\
35918.23089902530 & 35918.23089902530 & 355209.46891591826
\end{pmatrix}.
}
\end{equation}

\section{4. Normalized orbit-sum convention relevant for the current residual ansatz}

The current corrected residual ansatz is written in terms of normalized orbit sums
\[
\Phi_A,\qquad \Phi_E,\qquad \Phi_O,
\]
so if the orbit amplitudes \(a_X\) multiply normalized orbit vectors, then the shell amplitude per patch is
\[
\psi_\alpha=\frac{a_X}{\sqrt{n_X}}
\qquad
(\alpha\in X).
\]

In that convention, the quartic coefficient matrix entering
\[
F_4^{\mathrm{red}}=\frac14\sum_{X,Y}\bar M_{XY}r_Xr_Y,
\qquad
r_X=a_X^2,
\]
is
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
\bar M_{XY}
=
\frac{1}{n_X n_Y}
M^{\mathrm{raw}}_{XY}
=
\sum_c
\frac{N^{(c)}_{XY}}{n_X n_Y}u_c.
}
\end{equation}

Hence
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
\bar M
=
\begin{pmatrix}
8683.640035688459 & 9027.17290917827 & 2244.8894311890813\\
9027.17290917827 & 8683.640035688459 & 2244.8894311890813\\
2244.8894311890813 & 2244.8894311890813 & 5550.147951811223
\end{pmatrix}.
}
\end{equation}

This is the coefficient matrix compatible with the normalized-orbit reduced variables
appearing in
\[
\Psi(\delta)=\Psi_{\mathrm{BCC}}+\delta_A\Phi_A+\delta_E\Phi_E+\delta_O\Phi_O.
\]

\section{5. Residual-level quartic contribution}

Under the normalized orbit convention,
\begin{equation}
F_4^{\mathrm{red}}(r_A,r_E,r_O)
=
\frac14
\sum_{X,Y\in\{A,E,O\}}
\bar M_{XY}r_Xr_Y.
\end{equation}

Therefore the quartic residual contribution is
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
G_X^{(4)}(a_A,a_E,a_O)
=
a_X\sum_{Y\in\{A,E,O\}}\bar M_{XY}r_Y,
\qquad
r_Y=a_Y^2.
}
\end{equation}

Explicitly,
\begin{align}
G_A^{(4)}
&=
a_A\Bigl(
8683.640035688459\,r_A
+
9027.17290917827\,r_E
+
2244.8894311890813\,r_O
\Bigr),
\\
G_E^{(4)}
&=
a_E\Bigl(
9027.17290917827\,r_A
+
8683.640035688459\,r_E
+
2244.8894311890813\,r_O
\Bigr),
\\
G_O^{(4)}
&=
a_O\Bigl(
2244.8894311890813\,r_A
+
2244.8894311890813\,r_E
+
5550.147951811223\,r_O
\Bigr).
\end{align}

\section{6. Structural consequence: coarse four-class closure cannot break \(A/E\) symmetry}

From the exact count matrices,
\begin{equation}
N^{(c)}_{AA}=N^{(c)}_{EE},
\qquad
N^{(c)}_{AO}=N^{(c)}_{EO},
\qquad
N^{(c)}_{AE}=N^{(c)}_{EA},
\qquad
\forall c\in\{d,\mathrm{opp},\perp,\mathrm{obl}\}.
\end{equation}
Therefore
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
\bar M_{AA}=\bar M_{EE},
\qquad
\bar M_{AO}=\bar M_{EO},
\qquad
\bar M_{AE}=\bar M_{EA}.
}
\end{equation}

Hence the coarse four-class quartic sector is exactly invariant under
\[
A\leftrightarrow E.
\]

So the corrected branch asymmetry
\[
\delta_A^{\mathrm{final}}=+0.0096,
\qquad
\delta_E^{\mathrm{final}}=-0.0288
\]
cannot be explained by the coarse four-class quartic matrix alone.

The only possibilities are:

\begin{enumerate}
\item the quadratic orbit means already break that symmetry,
\[
\sigma_A\neq \sigma_E;
\]

\item higher reduced terms beyond coarse pair-class closure are relevant;

\item the true residual-level reduction must be refined to the sign-resolved suborbit basis
\[
(A_+,A_-,E_+,E_-,O_{++},O_{+-},O_{-+},O_{--}).
\]
\end{enumerate}

\section{7. Exact status after the combinatorial closure}

The coarse-orbit pair-class combinatorics is now completely closed.

What remains open is not
\[
N^{(c)}_{XY},
\]
but the next insertion step:
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
(\sigma_A,\sigma_E,\sigma_O)
\quad+\quad
\bar M
\quad\longrightarrow\quad
G_X^{(2)}+G_X^{(4)}
}
\end{equation}
inside the actual projected residual system.

If this still fails to account for the corrected branch geometry, then the next irreducible refinement is
not another pair-class kernel, but the sign-resolved suborbit quartic system.

\section{Final summary}

The exact ordered-pair class-count matrices of the coarse BCC orbit split are
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
N^{(d)}
=
\begin{pmatrix}

\end{pmatrix},
\quad

\begin{equation}
\boxed{

=

\end{pmatrix},
\quad
N^{(\mathrm{obl})}
=
\begin{pmatrix}

\end{pmatrix}.
}
\end{equation}

For the corrected four-class quartic data, the normalized coarse-orbit quartic matrix is
\begin{equation}

=
\begin{pmatrix}

Therefore the quartic contribution to the reduced residual system is now explicit:

G_X^{(4)}=a_X\sum_Y \bar M_{XY}r_Y.
\]

But this closure is still exactly \(A\leftrightarrow E\) symmetric.
So the corrected \(A/E\) asymmetry cannot come from the coarse four-class quartic sector alone.

Hence the next nonredundant step is sharply fixed:
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
\text{insert }(\sigma_A,\sigma_E,\sigma_O)\text{ and }\bar M\text{ into the residual equations,}
}
\end{equation}
and if that is still insufficient,
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
\text{upgrade from coarse }(A,E,O)\text{ to the sign-resolved suborbit quartic basis.}
}
\end{equation}

\section{Final summary}

\begin{equation}
\boxed{
\text{Current status: the coarse-orbit four-class combinatorics is now closed.}
}
\end{equation}

The frozen corrected residual stage keeps
\[
(\mu^2,\lambda,\gamma)=(0.26,-0.43,1.62),
\qquad

and the corrected quartic class data

u_d=2493.1087739398513,\quad
u_{\mathrm{opp}}=14874.171297437068,\quad

\]

For the coarse orbit split
\[
|A|=2,\qquad |E|=2,\qquad |O|=8,
\]
the exact ordered-pair count matrices are
\[
N^{(d)}
=
\begin{pmatrix}

=

\end{pmatrix},
\]

=

\end{pmatrix},
\qquad

Under the normalized orbit-sum convention, the corrected quartic matrix is

\bar M

inside the actual projected residual system.

\section{Coarse Residual Insertion of the Corrected Seed and the Necessity of \(A/E\) Quadratic Splitting}

\section{Short explanation}

At the current corrected residual stage, the correct reduced object is the projected residual map
\[
G(\delta)=\bigl(G_A(\delta),G_E(\delta),G_O(\delta)\bigr)^T,
\]
with frozen backend
\[
(\mu^2,\lambda,\gamma)=(0.26,-0.43,1.62)
\]
and frozen corrected seed
\[
\delta^{\mathrm{final}}=(0.0096,-0.0288,0.0096).
\]

The four-class quartic kernel is already fixed exactly by
\[
u_d=U_d,\qquad
u_{\mathrm{opp}}=U_{\mathrm{opp}},\qquad
u_\perp=U_\perp,\qquad
u_{\mathrm{obl}}=U_{\mathrm{obl}}.
\]

So the next nonredundant step is no longer combinatorics by itself, but insertion of the corrected seed into the class-resolved residual equations.

The main result is:

\begin{equation}
\boxed{
\text{if the coarse quadratic sector is }A/E\text{-symmetric, then the corrected seed cannot satisfy the coarse residual stationarity equations.}
}
\end{equation}

Hence a nonzero \(A/E\) quadratic splitting is necessary already at the coarse \((A,E,O)\) level.

\section{1. Coarse normalized quartic matrix}

For the normalized orbit-sum convention, the class-resolved quartic matrix is
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
\bar M
=
\begin{pmatrix}
\bar M_{AA} & \bar M_{AE} & \bar M_{AO}\\
\bar M_{EA} & \bar M_{EE} & \bar M_{EO}\\
\bar M_{OA} & \bar M_{OE} & \bar M_{OO}
\end{pmatrix}
=
\begin{pmatrix}
8683.640035688459 & 9027.17290917827 & 2244.8894311890813\\
9027.17290917827 & 8683.640035688459 & 2244.8894311890813\\
2244.8894311890813 & 2244.8894311890813 & 5550.147951811223
\end{pmatrix}.
}
\end{equation}

Equivalently, in terms of the four class means,
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
\bar M_{AA}=\bar M_{EE}=\frac{u_d+u_{\mathrm{opp}}}{2},
\qquad
\bar M_{AE}=\bar M_{EA}=u_\perp,
\qquad
\bar M_{AO}=\bar M_{EO}=u_{\mathrm{obl}},
}
\end{equation}
and
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
\bar M_{OO}
=
\frac{u_d+u_{\mathrm{opp}}+2u_\perp+4u_{\mathrm{obl}}}{8}.
}
\end{equation}

Thus the coarse quartic sector is exactly invariant under
\[
A\leftrightarrow E.
\]

\section{2. Coarse residual ansatz}

Write the coarse reduced residual system in the minimal diagonal quadratic form
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
G_X(a_A,a_E,a_O)
=
a_X
\left(
q_X+\sum_{Y\in\{A,E,O\}}\bar M_{XY}r_Y
\right),
\qquad
r_Y:=a_Y^2,
\qquad
X\in\{A,E,O\}.
}
\label{eq:coarse_residual_ansatz}
\end{equation}

Here \(q_A,q_E,q_O\) are the unknown effective coarse quadratic coefficients in the same normalization as \(\bar M\).

\paragraph{Important honesty note.}
These \(q_X\) are \emph{effective coefficients of the present coarse reduced ansatz}.
They should not be automatically identified with the numerically extracted orbit means
\[
\sigma_A,\sigma_E,\sigma_O
\]
unless the normalization map between the extractor output and \eqref{eq:coarse_residual_ansatz} is checked explicitly.

\section{3. Insert the corrected seed}

For the frozen corrected seed,
\begin{equation}
(a_A,a_E,a_O)=(0.0096,-0.0288,0.0096),
\end{equation}
so
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
r_A=r_O=\rho,
\qquad
r_E=9\rho,
\qquad
\rho:=(0.0096)^2=9.216\times 10^{-5}.
}
\end{equation}

Define the quartic brackets
\begin{equation}
Q_A:=\sum_Y \bar M_{AY}r_Y,
\qquad
Q_E:=\sum_Y \bar M_{EY}r_Y,
\qquad
Q_O:=\sum_Y \bar M_{OY}r_Y.
\end{equation}

Using the above \(\bar M\), one obtains
\begin{align}
Q_A
&=
\bar M_{AA}\rho+\bar M_{AE}(9\rho)+\bar M_{AO}\rho
=
8.4946715734562584,
\\
Q_E
&=
\bar M_{EA}\rho+\bar M_{EE}(9\rho)+\bar M_{EO}\rho
=
8.2413916564896905,
\\
Q_O
&=
\bar M_{OA}\rho+\bar M_{OE}(9\rho)+\bar M_{OO}\rho
=
2.5803917350227796.
\end{align}

Hence the quartic residual part at the corrected seed is
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
G^{(4)}(\delta^{\mathrm{final}})
=
\begin{pmatrix}
a_AQ_A\\
a_EQ_E\\
a_OQ_O
\end{pmatrix}
=
\begin{pmatrix}
0.08154884710518008\\
-0.23735207970690309\\
0.024771760656218685
\end{pmatrix}.
}
\end{equation}

Its norm is
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
\|G^{(4)}(\delta^{\mathrm{final}})\|
\approx 0.252190135277701.
}
\end{equation}

So the quartic contribution by itself is much larger than the actual corrected residual norm
\[
\|G(\delta^{\mathrm{final}})\|\approx 0.03791852545979427.
\]
Therefore substantial cancellation by the lower-order residual sector is necessary.

\section{4. Exact obstruction under \(A/E\)-symmetric quadratic sector}

Assume first the coarse quadratic sector is \(A/E\)-symmetric:
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
q_A=q_E=:q.
}
\end{equation}

If the corrected seed were an exact stationary point of the coarse residual system, then since
\[
a_A\neq 0,\qquad a_E\neq 0,
\]
we would need
\begin{equation}
q+Q_A=0,
\qquad
q+Q_E=0.
\end{equation}
Subtracting gives
\begin{equation}
Q_A-Q_E=0.
\end{equation}

But
\begin{equation}
Q_A-Q_E
=
(\bar M_{AA}-\bar M_{AE})(r_A-r_E).
\end{equation}
Now
\begin{equation}
\bar M_{AA}-\bar M_{AE}
=
8683.640035688459-9027.17290917827
=
-343.5328734898113\neq 0,
\end{equation}
and
\begin{equation}
r_A-r_E
=
\rho-9\rho
=
-8\rho
=
-0.00073728
\neq 0.
\end{equation}
Hence
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
Q_A-Q_E
=
0.25327991696656785
\neq 0.
}
\end{equation}

Therefore the corrected seed cannot satisfy the coarse residual stationarity equations under \(q_A=q_E\).

\begin{theorem}[Necessity of coarse \(A/E\) quadratic splitting]
For the coarse residual ansatz \eqref{eq:coarse_residual_ansatz} with the class-resolved quartic matrix \(\bar M\) above, the corrected seed
\[
(a_A,a_E,a_O)=(0.0096,-0.0288,0.0096)
\]
cannot be an exact stationary point if
\[
q_A=q_E.
\]
Equivalently, any exact coarse residual realization of the corrected seed requires
\[
\boxed{
q_A\neq q_E.
}
\]
\end{theorem}

\begin{proof}
The calculation above shows that exact stationarity would force
\[
Q_A=Q_E,
\]
but the corrected seed gives
\[
Q_A-Q_E=0.25327991696656785\neq 0.
\]
Hence \(q_A=q_E\) is impossible.
\end{proof}

\section{5. Exact required quadratic coefficients for the corrected seed}

If one insists that the corrected seed be an exact coarse stationary point, then the required quadratic coefficients are uniquely fixed by
\begin{equation}
q_A=-Q_A,\qquad q_E=-Q_E,\qquad q_O=-Q_O.
\end{equation}

Therefore
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
q_A^\star=-8.4946715734562584,
\qquad
q_E^\star=-8.2413916564896905,
\qquad
q_O^\star=-2.5803917350227796.
}
\end{equation}

In particular, the required coarse \(A/E\) splitting is
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
q_A^\star-q_E^\star
=
-(Q_A-Q_E)
=
-0.25327991696656785.
}
\end{equation}

Thus the corrected seed requires the quadratic sector to satisfy
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
q_A<q_E.
}
\end{equation}

\section{6. Comparison with the numerically extracted orbit asymmetry}

The corrected projection already yields orbit means
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
\sigma_A=-115.81015796064617,
\qquad
\sigma_E=-105.83201242243113,
\qquad
\sigma_O=-108.719022860933.
}
\end{equation}

So the numerical extractor already displays the same directional asymmetry
\[
\boxed{
\sigma_A<\sigma_E.
}
\]

This does \emph{not} by itself prove that
\[
q_A^\star-q_E^\star
=
\sigma_A-\sigma_E,
\]
because the normalization map between the extractor output and the coarse residual ansatz has not yet been closed.

However, it does show that the corrected branch is numerically aligned with the same \(A/E\)-splitting direction required by the coarse residual insertion.

\section{7. Sharp consequence}

The coarse class-resolved quartic closure is not the end of the story.
What it proves is more precise:

\begin{enumerate}
\item the corrected seed is incompatible with an \(A/E\)-symmetric coarse quadratic sector;
\item the corrected seed can be supported at coarse level only if a nonzero quadratic splitting
\[
q_A-q_E=-0.25327991696656785
\]
(in the coarse normalization) is present;
\item therefore the next reduced task is not more pair counting, but explicit insertion of the quadratic residual layer and its normalization map.
\end{enumerate}

So the next exact reduced system to write is
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
G_X(a_A,a_E,a_O)
=
a_X
\left(
q_X+\sum_Y \bar M_{XY}r_Y
\right)
+
G_X^{(\mathrm{rem})},
}
\end{equation}
where \(G_X^{(\mathrm{rem})}\) collects any still-unresolved residual pieces beyond the minimal coarse quadratic-plus-quartic truncation.

\section{Final summary}

At the corrected residual stage, the frozen seed
\[
(a_A,a_E,a_O)=(0.0096,-0.0288,0.0096)
\]
gives
\[
r_A=r_O=\rho,\qquad r_E=9\rho,\qquad \rho=9.216\times10^{-5}.
\]

Inserting this into the exact coarse four-class quartic matrix yields
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
Q_A=8.4946715734562584,\qquad
Q_E=8.2413916564896905,\qquad
Q_O=2.5803917350227796,
}
\end{equation}
and hence
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
G^{(4)}(\delta^{\mathrm{final}})
=
\begin{pmatrix}
0.08154884710518008\\
-0.23735207970690309\\
0.024771760656218685
\end{pmatrix}.
}
\end{equation}

Therefore the quartic contribution alone is not small; it must be cancelled by the quadratic residual layer.

More sharply, if the coarse quadratic sector is \(A/E\)-symmetric, then the corrected seed cannot be stationary, because exact stationarity would require
\[
Q_A=Q_E,
\]
but in fact
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
Q_A-Q_E=0.25327991696656785\neq 0.
}
\end{equation}

Hence a nonzero coarse \(A/E\) quadratic splitting is necessary:
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
q_A\neq q_E.
}
\end{equation}

The exact coarse stationary realization of the corrected seed requires
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
q_A^\star=-8.4946715734562584,\qquad
q_E^\star=-8.2413916564896905,\qquad
q_O^\star=-2.5803917350227796,
}
\end{equation}
so in particular
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
q_A^\star-q_E^\star=-0.25327991696656785<0.
}
\end{equation}

Thus the next nonredundant step is not another quartic combinatorial refinement.
It is the explicit identification and normalization of the quadratic residual layer
that produces the required \(A/E\) splitting.

\section{Final summary}

\begin{equation}
\boxed{
\text{Current status: coarse four-class quartic closure is inserted, and it already forces a nontrivial }A/E\text{ quadratic splitting.}
}
\end{equation}

The frozen corrected seed is
\[
(a_A,a_E,a_O)=(0.0096,-0.0288,0.0096),
\]
so
\[
r_A=r_O=\rho,\qquad r_E=9\rho,\qquad \rho=9.216\times 10^{-5}.
\]

Using the exact coarse normalized four-class quartic matrix
\[
\bar M
=
\begin{pmatrix}
8683.640035688459 & 9027.17290917827 & 2244.8894311890813\\
9027.17290917827 & 8683.640035688459 & 2244.8894311890813\\
2244.8894311890813 & 2244.8894311890813 & 5550.147951811223
\end{pmatrix},
\]
one obtains
\[
Q_A=8.4946715734562584,\qquad
Q_E=8.2413916564896905,\qquad
Q_O=2.5803917350227796,
\]
and therefore
\[
G^{(4)}(\delta^{\mathrm{final}})
=
\begin{pmatrix}
0.08154884710518008\\
-0.23735207970690309\\
0.024771760656218685
\end{pmatrix}.
\]

Hence the quartic contribution is not small by itself and must be cancelled by the lower-order residual sector.

Most importantly, if the coarse quadratic sector were \(A/E\)-symmetric, then exact stationarity would force
\[
Q_A=Q_E,
\]
but in fact
\[
Q_A-Q_E=0.25327991696656785\neq 0.
\]

Therefore the corrected seed cannot be supported by an \(A/E\)-symmetric coarse residual model.
A nonzero quadratic splitting is necessary:
\[
\boxed{
q_A\neq q_E.
}
\]

The exact coarse stationary realization of the corrected seed requires
\[
q_A^\star=-8.4946715734562584,\qquad
q_E^\star=-8.2413916564896905,\qquad
q_O^\star=-2.5803917350227796,
\]
so that
\[
\boxed{
q_A^\star-q_E^\star=-0.25327991696656785<0.
}
\]

Thus the next exact reduced task is no longer pair counting.
It is:
\[
\boxed{
\text{identify the quadratic residual layer and its normalization map.}
}
\]

\begin{equation}
\boxed{
\text{Goal C: complete the corrected residual branch by closing the quadratic-plus-quartic residual system.}
}
\end{equation}
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
\text{Goal M: determine the actual coarse quadratic coefficients }(q_A,q_E,q_O)\text{ from the extractor output.}
}
\end{equation}
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
\text{Goal S: write and test the explicit normalized map from extracted orbit means to the coarse residual coefficients.}
}
\end{equation}

\section{Quadratic Normalization Audit Beyond the Naive Identification \(q_X=\sigma_X\)}

\section{Short explanation}

At the corrected residual stage, the frozen data are:

\begin{equation}
\boxed{
\delta^{\mathrm{final}}=(0.0096,-0.0288,0.0096),
\qquad
\|G(\delta^{\mathrm{final}})\|
\approx
0.03791852545979427.
}
\end{equation}

The corrected projection extractor gives
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
\sigma_0=-109.41971030446824,
\qquad
\eta=-6.502583100163014,
}
\end{equation}
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
\sigma_A=-115.81015796064617,
\quad
\sigma_E=-105.83201242243113,
\quad
\sigma_O=-108.719022860933,
}
\end{equation}
and the four exact quartic class means
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
U_d=2493.1087739398513,
\quad
U_{\mathrm{opp}}=14874.171297437068,
\quad
U_\perp=9027.17290917827,
\quad
U_{\mathrm{obl}}=2244.8894311890813.
}
\end{equation}

The key question is now:
\[
\boxed{
\text{how do the extracted orbit means }(\sigma_A,\sigma_E,\sigma_O)
\text{ map to the actual coarse quadratic residual layer }(q_A,q_E,q_O)?
}
\]

The main result of this section is negative but decisive:

\begin{equation}
\boxed{
q_X=\sigma_X\ \text{fails strongly,}
}
\end{equation}
and even the more general two-parameter \((\sigma_0,\eta)\)-based linear shell map fails.
Therefore the correct next object is the full local residual Jacobian
\[
J_{XY}=\partial_{\delta_Y}G_X|_{\delta^{\mathrm{final}}},
\]
not another compression to \((\sigma_0,\eta)\) or a single orbitwise scale factor.

\section{1. Required coarse quadratic coefficients from the corrected seed}

From the already-constructed coarse four-class quartic insertion, the normalized quartic matrix is
\begin{equation}
\bar M
=
\begin{pmatrix}
8683.640035688459 & 9027.17290917827 & 2244.8894311890813\\
9027.17290917827 & 8683.640035688459 & 2244.8894311890813\\
2244.8894311890813 & 2244.8894311890813 & 5550.147951811223
\end{pmatrix}.
\end{equation}

For
\[
(a_A,a_E,a_O)=(0.0096,-0.0288,0.0096),
\]
we have
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
r_A=r_O=\rho,\qquad r_E=9\rho,\qquad \rho=(0.0096)^2=9.216\times 10^{-5}.
}
\end{equation}

Hence the quartic brackets are
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
Q_A=8.4946715734562584,\qquad
Q_E=8.2413916564896905,\qquad
Q_O=2.5803917350227796.
}
\end{equation}

Therefore, in the minimal coarse ansatz
\begin{equation}
G_X(a_A,a_E,a_O)
=
a_X\left(q_X+\sum_Y \bar M_{XY}r_Y\right),
\end{equation}
the exact stationary realization of the corrected seed requires
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
q_A^\star=-Q_A=-8.4946715734562584,
}
\end{equation}
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
q_E^\star=-Q_E=-8.2413916564896905,
}
\end{equation}
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
q_O^\star=-Q_O=-2.5803917350227796.
}
\end{equation}

So the target quadratic layer that must be explained is numerically fixed.

\section{2. Canonical direct identification and why it fails}

The residual-based reduced object is
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
G(\delta)
=
\begin{pmatrix}
G_A(\delta)\\
G_E(\delta)\\
G_O(\delta)
\end{pmatrix}
=
\begin{pmatrix}
\Re\langle \Phi_A,R[\Psi(\delta)]\rangle_\Omega\\
\Re\langle \Phi_E,R[\Psi(\delta)]\rangle_\Omega\\
\Re\langle \Phi_O,R[\Psi(\delta)]\rangle_\Omega
\end{pmatrix}.
}
\end{equation}

Suppose, for the moment, that:

\begin{enumerate}
\item the extracted shell coefficients \(h_\alpha\) are the linear coefficients of the same reduced coordinates \(\delta_A,\delta_E,\delta_O\);
\item the orbit basis \(\Phi_A,\Phi_E,\Phi_O\) is normalized compatibly with the extractor convention;
\item the quadratic reduced residual layer is diagonal in the coarse orbit basis.
\end{enumerate}

Then the canonical linear projection gives
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
G_X^{(2)}=\sigma_X\,a_X,
}
\end{equation}
so that the direct identification would be
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
q_X^{\mathrm{can}}=\sigma_X.
}
\end{equation}

Numerically this predicts
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
q_A^{\mathrm{can}}=-115.81015796064617,\qquad
q_E^{\mathrm{can}}=-105.83201242243113,\qquad
q_O^{\mathrm{can}}=-108.719022860933.
}
\end{equation}

But the corrected seed requires instead
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
q_A^\star=-8.4946715734562584,\qquad
q_E^\star=-8.2413916564896905,\qquad
q_O^\star=-2.5803917350227796.
}
\end{equation}

Hence the ratios are
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
\frac{q_A^\star}{\sigma_A}=0.07334996966624345,\qquad
\frac{q_E^\star}{\sigma_E}=0.07787238915569299,\qquad
\frac{q_O^\star}{\sigma_O}=0.023734500799583762.
}
\end{equation}

These are not equal.
Therefore even a single common scale factor fails.

\begin{theorem}[Failure of the direct normalization map]
The corrected seed data are incompatible with the direct identification
\[
q_X=\sigma_X
\]
and also incompatible with any single common rescaling
\[
q_X=\zeta\,\sigma_X
\]
of the extracted orbit means.
\end{theorem}

\begin{proof}
Direct comparison shows
\[
q_X^\star\neq \sigma_X
\]
by more than an order of magnitude in each orbit.
Moreover the three ratios
\[
q_A^\star/\sigma_A,\quad q_E^\star/\sigma_E,\quad q_O^\star/\sigma_O
\]
are unequal, so no common \(\zeta\) can satisfy
\[
q_X^\star=\zeta\,\sigma_X
\]
simultaneously for \(X=A,E,O\).
\end{proof}

\section{3. Two-parameter \texorpdfstring{$(\sigma_0,\eta)$}{(sigma0,eta)} map and its failure}

The extractor formulas give
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
\sigma_A=\sigma_0+\eta,\qquad
\sigma_E=\sigma_0-\frac{\eta}{2},\qquad
\sigma_O=\sigma_0-\frac{\eta}{8}.
}
\end{equation}

Therefore the most natural two-parameter linear shell model is
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
q_A=c_0\sigma_0+c_2\eta,\qquad
q_E=c_0\sigma_0-\frac12 c_2\eta,\qquad
q_O=c_0\sigma_0-\frac18 c_2\eta.
}
\label{eq:two_param_map}
\end{equation}

Fitting \(q_A^\star\) and \(q_E^\star\) determines
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
c_0=0.07609066293733878,
\qquad
c_2=0.025967108051795404.
}
\end{equation}

This predicts
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
q_O^{\mathrm{pred}}
=
c_0\sigma_0-\frac18 c_2\eta
=
-8.304711635731334.
}
\end{equation}

But the corrected seed requires
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
q_O^\star=-2.5803917350227796.
}
\end{equation}

So the defect is
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
q_O^{\mathrm{pred}}-q_O^\star=-5.724319900708554.
}
\end{equation}

This is far too large to be attributed to the small corrected residual norm
\[
\|G(\delta^{\mathrm{final}})\|\approx 0.03791852545979427.
\]

\begin{theorem}[Failure of the \((\sigma_0,\eta)\)-only quadratic map]
The required corrected-seed quadratic coefficients \((q_A^\star,q_E^\star,q_O^\star)\) cannot be generated by any linear map depending only on the two shell coefficients \((\sigma_0,\eta)\) in the canonical orbit pattern
\[
(\sigma_0+\eta,\ \sigma_0-\eta/2,\ \sigma_0-\eta/8).
\]
\end{theorem}

\begin{proof}
The two-parameter ansatz \eqref{eq:two_param_map} is uniquely fixed by \(q_A^\star\) and \(q_E^\star\), but its prediction for \(q_O\) differs from the required corrected value by
\[
-5.724319900708554,
\]
which is not compatible with a merely small correction.
Therefore no such two-parameter map can reproduce the corrected quadratic layer.
\end{proof}

\section{4. Honest interpretation}

The files already warn that the extracted coefficients
\[
(\sigma_0,\eta,U_d,U_{\mathrm{opp}},U_\perp,U_{\mathrm{obl}})
\]
must be interpreted honestly as
\[
\boxed{
\text{effective reduced coefficients of the current solver family,}
}
\]
not yet theorem-level coefficients of a fully closed microscopic functional.

The present audit now sharpens that warning.

What fails is not only the old quartic compression
\[
(u,v,\kappa),
\]
but also the naive assumption that the corrected quadratic residual layer is directly encoded by the extracted orbit means.

Therefore at least one of the following must be true:

\begin{enumerate}
\item the variables \((\delta_A,\delta_E,\delta_O)\) are not the canonically normalized orbit amplitudes used by the linear extractor;
\item the local quadratic residual layer is not diagonal in the coarse orbit basis;
\item the \(\{1,P_2\}\) shell truncation is insufficient for the corrected branch, and the remainder
\[
h_\alpha^{\mathrm{rem}}
\]
is quantitatively important;
\item the residual reduction around the corrected branch involves a nontrivial Jacobian between extraction coordinates and correction coordinates.
\end{enumerate}

\section{5. Minimal honest coarse quadratic layer}

The failure of both \(q_X=\sigma_X\) and the \((\sigma_0,\eta)\)-map forces the following conclusion.

The minimal honest linearized reduced residual layer must be
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
G_X^{(2)}(\delta)
=
\sum_{Y\in\{A,E,O\}} L_{XY}\,\delta_Y,
\qquad
L\in M_3(\mathbb R),
}
\end{equation}
rather than
\[
G_X^{(2)}=q_X\delta_X
\]
with \(q_X\) obtained from a direct shell-average identification.

At the corrected seed,
\[
\delta^{\mathrm{final}}=(0.0096,-0.0288,0.0096),
\]
the local balance must satisfy
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
L\,\delta^{\mathrm{final}}
+
\begin{pmatrix}
a_AQ_A\\
a_EQ_E\\
a_OQ_O
\end{pmatrix}
=
G(\delta^{\mathrm{final}})+R_{\ge 5},
}
\end{equation}
where
\[
\left\|
\begin{pmatrix}
a_AQ_A\\
a_EQ_E\\
a_OQ_O
\end{pmatrix}
\right\|
\approx 0.252190135277701
\]
and
\[
\|G(\delta^{\mathrm{final}})\|
\approx 0.03791852545979427.
\]

Thus the linear layer must cancel most of the quartic vector locally.
This is an intrinsically matrix-valued statement, not a scalar orbit-mean statement.

\section{6. Exact next executable object: the residual Jacobian}

Since the correct reduced object is the residual map itself, the next exact finite object is
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
J_{XY}
:=
\left.
\frac{\partial G_X}{\partial \delta_Y}
\right|_{\delta=\delta^{\mathrm{final}}},
\qquad
X,Y\in\{A,E,O\}.
}
\end{equation}

This is the actual quadratic normalization map.

A centered finite-difference realization is
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
J_{XY}
\approx
\frac{
G_X(\delta^{\mathrm{final}}+\varepsilon e_Y)
-
G_X(\delta^{\mathrm{final}}-\varepsilon e_Y)
}{
2\varepsilon
},
}
\end{equation}
where \(e_Y\) is the unit vector in the \(Y\)-direction and \(\varepsilon\) is chosen inside the geometry-preserving local window.

Once \(J\) is known, the corrected local reduced model becomes
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
G(\delta)
=
G(\delta^{\mathrm{final}})
+
J(\delta-\delta^{\mathrm{final}})
+
G^{(4)}(\delta)
-
G^{(4)}(\delta^{\mathrm{final}})
+
\cdots.
}
\end{equation}

This is the first reduced model that is guaranteed to live on the correct residual object.

\section{Final summary}

The corrected branch data force the following sharp conclusion.

The direct identification
\[
q_X=\sigma_X
\]
fails strongly.

Even the more flexible two-parameter shell-linear map built from
\[
(\sigma_0,\eta)
\]
fails:
after fitting \(q_A^\star\) and \(q_E^\star\), it predicts
\[
q_O^{\mathrm{pred}}=-8.304711635731334,
\]
whereas the corrected seed requires
\[
q_O^\star=-2.5803917350227796.
\]

Therefore the quadratic residual layer is not encoded by the extracted shell means through any naive direct map.

The minimal honest conclusion is:
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
\text{the corrected coarse quadratic layer is matrix-valued and must be extracted as the local Jacobian of the residual map itself.}
}
\end{equation}

So the next exact reduced object is
\[
\boxed{
J_{XY}
=
\partial_{\delta_Y}G_X|_{\delta^{\mathrm{final}}},
}
\]
not another compression of \((\sigma_A,\sigma_E,\sigma_O)\).

\section{Final summary}

\begin{equation}
\boxed{
\text{Current status: the quartic four-class closure is in place, and the next obstruction is now the quadratic normalization map.}
}
\end{equation}

The corrected branch is fixed by
\[
\delta^{\mathrm{final}}=(0.0096,-0.0288,0.0096),
\qquad
\|G(\delta^{\mathrm{final}})\|\approx 0.03791852545979427.
\]

The corrected extractor gives
\[
\sigma_0=-109.41971030446824,\qquad
\eta=-6.502583100163014,
\]
\[
\sigma_A=-115.81015796064617,\quad
\sigma_E=-105.83201242243113,\quad
\sigma_O=-108.719022860933,
\]
and the exact four-class quartic data
\[
U_d=2493.1087739398513,\quad
U_{\mathrm{opp}}=14874.171297437068,\quad
U_\perp=9027.17290917827,\quad
U_{\mathrm{obl}}=2244.8894311890813.
\]

The corrected seed requires the coarse quadratic coefficients
\[
q_A^\star=-8.4946715734562584,\qquad
q_E^\star=-8.2413916564896905,\qquad
q_O^\star=-2.5803917350227796.
\]

However, the direct map
\[
q_X=\sigma_X
\]
fails strongly, and even a general two-parameter shell-linear map based only on
\[
(\sigma_0,\eta)
\]
fails.
So the quadratic residual layer is not captured by a naive shell-average identification.

Therefore the next honest reduced object is the local residual Jacobian
\[
\boxed{
J_{XY}
=
\left.\frac{\partial G_X}{\partial \delta_Y}\right|_{\delta=\delta^{\mathrm{final}}}.
}
\]

This Jacobian is the actual normalization map between the corrected local coordinates
\[
(\delta_A,\delta_E,\delta_O)
\]
and the true quadratic residual layer.

\begin{equation}
\boxed{
\text{Goal C: close the corrected residual branch by extracting the local quadratic Jacobian.}
}
\end{equation}
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
\text{Goal M: replace the failed identifications }q_X=\sigma_X\text{ and }q_X=q_X(\sigma_0,\eta)\text{ by }J_{XY}. 
}
\end{equation}
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
\text{Goal S: evaluate }G(\delta^{\mathrm{final}}\pm \varepsilon e_Y)\text{ and build the centered-difference Jacobian.}
}
\end{equation}
