\section{Local Residual Jacobian Audit at the Corrected Seed}

\section{Short explanation}

The corrected reduced object is the projected residual map
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
G(\delta)
=
\begin{pmatrix}
G_A(\delta)\\
G_E(\delta)\\
G_O(\delta)
\end{pmatrix},
\qquad
\delta=(\delta_A,\delta_E,\delta_O),
}
\end{equation}
with frozen corrected seed
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
\delta^{\mathrm{final}}=(0.0096,-0.0288,0.0096).
}
\end{equation}

The next exact local object is therefore not another shell-average coefficient,
but the Jacobian
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
J_{XY}
:=
\left.
\frac{\partial G_X}{\partial \delta_Y}
\right|_{\delta=\delta^{\mathrm{final}}},
\qquad
X,Y\in\{A,E,O\}.
}
\end{equation}

What can already be closed exactly is the quartic contribution \(J^{(4)}\).
What remains to be extracted numerically is the full residual Jacobian \(J\) itself.

\section{1. Exact quartic reduced residual and its Jacobian}

From the corrected four-class coarse closure, the normalized quartic residual model is
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
G_X^{(4)}(a_A,a_E,a_O)
=
a_X\sum_{Y\in\{A,E,O\}}\bar M_{XY}r_Y,
\qquad
r_Y:=a_Y^2,
}
\end{equation}
with
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
\bar M
=
\begin{pmatrix}
8683.640035688459 & 9027.17290917827 & 2244.8894311890813\\
9027.17290917827 & 8683.640035688459 & 2244.8894311890813\\
2244.8894311890813 & 2244.8894311890813 & 5550.147951811223
\end{pmatrix}.
}
\end{equation}

Define
\begin{equation}
Q_X(a):=\sum_Y \bar M_{XY}a_Y^2.
\end{equation}
Then
\begin{equation}
G_X^{(4)}(a)=a_XQ_X(a).
\end{equation}

Therefore the exact quartic Jacobian is
\begin{align}
J^{(4)}_{XZ}(a)
&:=
\frac{\partial G_X^{(4)}}{\partial a_Z}
=
\frac{\partial}{\partial a_Z}
\left(
a_X\sum_Y \bar M_{XY}a_Y^2
\right)
\\
&=
\delta_{XZ}\sum_Y \bar M_{XY}a_Y^2
+
2a_X\bar M_{XZ}a_Z.
\end{align}

Equivalently,
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
J^{(4)}_{XZ}(a)
=
\delta_{XZ}Q_X(a)+2a_X\bar M_{XZ}a_Z.
}
\label{eq:J4_general}
\end{equation}

\section{2. Exact evaluation at the corrected seed}

Insert
\[
a_A=0.0096,\qquad
a_E=-0.0288,\qquad
a_O=0.0096.
\]
Then
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
r_A=r_O=\rho,\qquad
r_E=9\rho,\qquad
\rho=(0.0096)^2=9.216\times10^{-5}.
}
\end{equation}

The quartic brackets become
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
Q_A=8.4946715734562584,\qquad
Q_E=8.2413916564896905,\qquad
Q_O=2.5803917350227796.
}
\end{equation}

Hence the quartic residual vector at the corrected seed is
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
G^{(4)}(\delta^{\mathrm{final}})
=
\begin{pmatrix}
0.08154884710518008\\
-0.23735207970690309\\
0.024771760656218685
\end{pmatrix},
}
\end{equation}
with norm
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
\|G^{(4)}(\delta^{\mathrm{final}})\|
\approx
0.252190135277701.
}
\end{equation}

Now use \eqref{eq:J4_general}. One obtains
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
J^{(4)}(\delta^{\mathrm{final}})
=
\begin{pmatrix}
10.095240102467715 & -4.991665534361308 & 0.41377801570768584\\
-4.991665534361308 & 22.64650843997611 & -1.2413340471230576\\
0.41377801570768584 & -1.2413340471230576 & 3.6033950146440785
\end{pmatrix}.
}
\end{equation}

This matrix is fully explicit and already theorem-level closed at the reduced coarse four-class level.

\section{3. Important structural consequence}

Although the coarse quartic matrix \(\bar M\) is exactly \(A\leftrightarrow E\) symmetric,
its local Jacobian at the corrected asymmetric seed is not.

Indeed,
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
J^{(4)}_{AA}\neq J^{(4)}_{EE},
\qquad
J^{(4)}_{AO}\neq J^{(4)}_{EO},
}
\end{equation}
because the evaluation point itself satisfies
\[
a_A\neq a_E.
\]

So the correct local statement is sharper than the earlier coarse-matrix statement:

\begin{equation}
\boxed{
\text{the quartic coefficient matrix is }A/E\text{-symmetric, but the local quartic linearization at the corrected seed is already }A/E\text{-asymmetric.}
}
\end{equation}

This means that the true local reduced audit must be formulated directly at Jacobian level.

\section{4. Full local residual Taylor model}

Write the full corrected residual map near the corrected seed as
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
G(\delta^{\mathrm{final}}+\Delta)
=
G_f
+
J_f\,\Delta
+
\frac12\,\mathcal H_f[\Delta,\Delta]
+
O(\|\Delta\|^3),
}
\end{equation}
where
\[
G_f:=G(\delta^{\mathrm{final}}),
\qquad
J_f:=DG(\delta^{\mathrm{final}}).
\]

If we split
\[
G=G^{(2)}+G^{(4)}+G^{(\ge5)},
\]
then
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
J_f
=
J_f^{(2)}
+
J_f^{(4)}
+
J_f^{(\ge5)}.
}
\end{equation}

The exact part already known is
\[
J_f^{(4)}=J^{(4)}(\delta^{\mathrm{final}}).
\]
The actual unknown is therefore the remainder
\[
J_f^{(2)}+J_f^{(\ge5)}.
\]

\section{5. Why the Jacobian, not the shell means, is the correct next object}

The corrected extractor produces
\[
(\sigma_0,\eta),\qquad
(\sigma_A,\sigma_E,\sigma_O),
\]
but these were already identified as effective reduced coefficients of the current solver family, not yet a closed microscopic functional coefficient sheet.

The present local audit now sharpens that honesty condition.

The quantities
\[
\sigma_A,\sigma_E,\sigma_O
\]
do not determine the actual linearized corrected residual map by themselves.
What determines the local reduced dynamics is
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
J_f
=
\left.
\frac{\partial G}{\partial \delta}
\right|_{\delta=\delta^{\mathrm{final}}}.
}
\end{equation}

Therefore the correct reduced local Newton/Gauss--Newton object is \(J_f\), not a diagonal or orbitwise scalar approximation built from shell means.

\section{6. Coordinate-basis centered-difference extraction}

The first exact executable realization is the centered-difference stencil in the coordinate basis
\[
e_A=(1,0,0),\qquad
e_E=(0,1,0),\qquad
e_O=(0,0,1).
\]

For a sufficiently small admissible \(\varepsilon>0\),
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
J_{\cdot Y}
\approx
\frac{
G(\delta^{\mathrm{final}}+\varepsilon e_Y)
-
G(\delta^{\mathrm{final}}-\varepsilon e_Y)
}{
2\varepsilon
},
\qquad
Y\in\{A,E,O\}.
}
\label{eq:J_coord_fd}
\end{equation}

This requires six residual evaluations around the frozen corrected seed.

\section{7. Geometry-adapted Jacobian audit}

Because the admissible local valley is extremely narrow and follows the sign pattern
\[
(+,-,+),
\]
the more honest basis is the orthonormal adapted basis
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
t=\frac{1}{\sqrt{11}}(1,-3,1),
\qquad
n_1=\frac{1}{\sqrt2}(1,0,-1),
\qquad
n_2=\frac{1}{\sqrt{22}}(3,2,3).
}
\end{equation}

Here \(t\) is the corrected-branch tangent direction, while \(n_1,n_2\) span the local transverse plane.

Then define the adapted Jacobian columns by
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
\widetilde J_{\cdot k}
\approx
\frac{
G(\delta^{\mathrm{final}}+\varepsilon b_k)
-
G(\delta^{\mathrm{final}}-\varepsilon b_k)
}{
2\varepsilon
},
\qquad
b_1=t,\ b_2=n_1,\ b_3=n_2.
}
\label{eq:J_adapted_fd}
\end{equation}

If
\[
B=(t\,|\,n_1\,|\,n_2)
\]
is the orthogonal basis matrix, then
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
J\approx \widetilde J\,B^T.
}
\end{equation}

\paragraph{Why this is better.}
The coordinate-basis stencil \eqref{eq:J_coord_fd} is mathematically correct, but the adapted stencil \eqref{eq:J_adapted_fd} resolves directly:

\begin{enumerate}
\item the longitudinal corrected-branch response \(Jt\),
\item the two geometry-breaking transverse responses \(Jn_1\) and \(Jn_2\).
\end{enumerate}

This is the correct local certificate if the admissible corrected valley is truly narrow.

\section{8. Admissibility protocol for the finite-difference radius}

The local stencil points must stay inside the geometry-admissible domain.
Operationally, each test point must satisfy the same corrected reduced constraints used in the residual-refinement program:
\[
\texttt{shell\_fraction}\ge s_\star,
\qquad
\texttt{orbit\_balance}\ge b_\star,
\]
with operational thresholds
\[
s_\star=0.90,
\qquad
b_\star=0.88.
\]

Therefore \(\varepsilon\) must be chosen by backtracking:
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
\varepsilon\downarrow \varepsilon/2
\quad\text{until all six stencil points are admissible.}
}
\end{equation}

This is not a numerical convenience.
It is required because the corrected reduced object is only meaningful inside the geometry-preserving region.

\section{9. First local consistency check}

Once the full \(J_f\) is extracted, the first local consistency test is
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
J_f-J_f^{(4)}.
}
\end{equation}

If this difference is small, then the corrected branch is already largely controlled by the coarse four-class quartic closure.

If it is not small, then the unresolved local structure is necessarily coming from
\[
J_f^{(2)}+J_f^{(\ge5)},
\]
i.e. from the quadratic normalization layer and/or higher reduced residual terms.

This gives the first honest yes/no test for whether one must immediately upgrade to a sign-resolved suborbit residual model.

\section{Final summary}

The correct next reduced object is the full local residual Jacobian
\[
J_f=
\left.
\frac{\partial G}{\partial \delta}
\right|_{\delta=\delta^{\mathrm{final}}}.
\]

What is already closed exactly is its quartic piece:
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
J^{(4)}(\delta^{\mathrm{final}})
=
\begin{pmatrix}
10.095240102467715 & -4.991665534361308 & 0.41377801570768584\\
-4.991665534361308 & 22.64650843997611 & -1.2413340471230576\\
0.41377801570768584 & -1.2413340471230576 & 3.6033950146440785
\end{pmatrix}.
}
\end{equation}

Thus the next executable audit is finite and explicit:

\begin{enumerate}
\item choose an admissible \(\varepsilon\),
\item evaluate \(G\) at six centered stencil points,
\item reconstruct \(J_f\),
\item compare \(J_f\) with the exact quartic piece \(J_f^{(4)}\).
\end{enumerate}

That comparison, not another shell-average compression, is the correct local closure test.

\section{Final summary}

\begin{equation}
\boxed{
\text{Current status: the next exact reduced object is the local residual Jacobian at the frozen corrected seed.}
}
\end{equation}

The corrected seed and reduced object are fixed as
\[
\delta^{\mathrm{final}}=(0.0096,-0.0288,0.0096),
\qquad
G(\delta)=\bigl(G_A(\delta),G_E(\delta),G_O(\delta)\bigr)^T.
\]

The corrected extractor outputs
\[
(\sigma_0,\eta)=(-109.41971030446824,-6.502583100163014),
\]
\[
(\sigma_A,\sigma_E,\sigma_O)=(-115.81015796064617,-105.83201242243113,-108.719022860933),
\]
but these are only effective reduced coefficients of the current solver family and do not yet determine the true local linearization.

The exact quartic reduced residual is already known:
\[
G_X^{(4)}(a)=a_X\sum_Y \bar M_{XY}a_Y^2,
\]
with
\[
\bar M
=
\begin{pmatrix}
8683.640035688459 & 9027.17290917827 & 2244.8894311890813\\
9027.17290917827 & 8683.640035688459 & 2244.8894311890813\\
2244.8894311890813 & 2244.8894311890813 & 5550.147951811223
\end{pmatrix}.
\]

Therefore its exact Jacobian contribution is
\[
J^{(4)}_{XZ}
=
\delta_{XZ}Q_X+2a_X\bar M_{XZ}a_Z.
\]

At the corrected seed this gives
\[
\boxed{
J^{(4)}(\delta^{\mathrm{final}})
=
\begin{pmatrix}
10.095240102467715 & -4.991665534361308 & 0.41377801570768584\\
-4.991665534361308 & 22.64650843997611 & -1.2413340471230576\\
0.41377801570768584 & -1.2413340471230576 & 3.6033950146440785
\end{pmatrix}.
}
\]

Hence the remaining unknown is not the quartic local linearization itself, but the difference
\[
J_f-J_f^{(4)},
\qquad
J_f=\left.\frac{\partial G}{\partial \delta}\right|_{\delta=\delta^{\mathrm{final}}}.
\]

The correct next executable audit is therefore:
\[
\boxed{
J_{\cdot Y}
\approx
\frac{
G(\delta^{\mathrm{final}}+\varepsilon e_Y)-G(\delta^{\mathrm{final}}-\varepsilon e_Y)
}{
2\varepsilon
},
\qquad
Y\in\{A,E,O\},
}
\]
or, more honestly, the adapted-basis version along
\[
t=\frac{1}{\sqrt{11}}(1,-3,1),\qquad
n_1=\frac{1}{\sqrt2}(1,0,-1),\qquad
n_2=\frac{1}{\sqrt{22}}(3,2,3).
\]

\begin{equation}
\boxed{
\text{Goal C: close the corrected local reduced model at Jacobian level.}
}
\end{equation}
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
\text{Goal M: extract the full residual Jacobian }J_f\text{ and compare it to the exact quartic piece }J_f^{(4)}.
}
\end{equation}
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
\text{Goal S: decide from }J_f-J_f^{(4)}\text{ whether coarse four-class closure is locally sufficient or whether sign-resolved suborbit refinement is unavoidable.}
}
\end{equation}

%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%

\section{Jacobian-Level Local Certificate at the Corrected Seed}

\section{Short explanation}

The corrected reduced object is the projected residual map
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
G(\delta)
=
\begin{pmatrix}
G_A(\delta)\\
G_E(\delta)\\
G_O(\delta)
\end{pmatrix},
\qquad
\delta=(\delta_A,\delta_E,\delta_O),
}
\end{equation}
with frozen corrected seed
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
\delta_f:=\delta^{\mathrm{final}}=(0.0096,-0.0288,0.0096).
}
\end{equation}

The next exact local object is therefore
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
J_f
:=
DG(\delta_f)
=
\left.
\frac{\partial G}{\partial \delta}
\right|_{\delta=\delta_f}.
}
\end{equation}

The quartic part of this Jacobian is already exactly computable from the class-resolved coarse four-class closure, and provides the natural certificate baseline for the full finite audit.

\section{1. Exact quartic reduced residual}

At the coarse normalized-orbit level,
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
G_X^{(4)}(a)
=
a_X\sum_{Y\in\{A,E,O\}}\bar M_{XY}a_Y^2,
\qquad
a=(a_A,a_E,a_O),
}
\end{equation}
with
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
\bar M
=
\begin{pmatrix}
8683.640035688459 & 9027.17290917827 & 2244.8894311890813\\
9027.17290917827 & 8683.640035688459 & 2244.8894311890813\\
2244.8894311890813 & 2244.8894311890813 & 5550.147951811223
\end{pmatrix}.
}
\end{equation}

Define
\begin{equation}
Q_X(a):=\sum_Y \bar M_{XY}a_Y^2.
\end{equation}
Then
\begin{equation}
G^{(4)}(a)
=
\begin{pmatrix}
a_AQ_A(a)\\
a_EQ_E(a)\\
a_OQ_O(a)
\end{pmatrix}.
\end{equation}

At the corrected seed
\[
a_A=0.0096,\qquad
a_E=-0.0288,\qquad
a_O=0.0096,
\]
so
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
r_A=r_O=\rho,\qquad r_E=9\rho,\qquad \rho=(0.0096)^2=9.216\times10^{-5}.
}
\end{equation}

Hence
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
Q_A=8.4946715734562584,\qquad
Q_E=8.2413916564896905,\qquad
Q_O=2.5803917350227796.
}
\end{equation}

Therefore
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
G^{(4)}(\delta_f)
=
\begin{pmatrix}
0.08154884710518008\\
-0.23735207970690309\\
0.024771760656218685
\end{pmatrix},
}
\end{equation}
with
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
\|G^{(4)}(\delta_f)\|
\approx
0.252190135277701.
}
\end{equation}

\section{2. Exact quartic Jacobian}

Differentiate
\[
G_X^{(4)}(a)=a_X\sum_Y \bar M_{XY}a_Y^2.
\]
Then
\begin{align}
J^{(4)}_{XZ}(a)
&:=
\frac{\partial G_X^{(4)}}{\partial a_Z}
\\
&=
\delta_{XZ}\sum_Y \bar M_{XY}a_Y^2
+
2a_X\bar M_{XZ}a_Z.
\end{align}
So
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
J^{(4)}_{XZ}(a)
=
\delta_{XZ}Q_X(a)+2a_X\bar M_{XZ}a_Z.
}
\label{eq:J4_exact_general}
\end{equation}

At the corrected seed this becomes
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
J_f^{(4)}:=J^{(4)}(\delta_f)
=
\begin{pmatrix}
10.095240102467715 & -4.991665534361308 & 0.41377801570768584\\
-4.991665534361308 & 22.64650843997611 & -1.2413340471230576\\
0.41377801570768584 & -1.2413340471230576 & 3.6033950146440785
\end{pmatrix}.
}
\end{equation}

This matrix is symmetric. Its eigenvalues are
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
\lambda_1^{(4)}\approx 3.5212959,\qquad
\lambda_2^{(4)}\approx 8.35219818,\qquad
\lambda_3^{(4)}\approx 24.47164948.
}
\end{equation}
Hence
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
J_f^{(4)}>0.
}
\end{equation}

So the quartic local linearization is already coercive.

\section{3. Euler identity and the exact quartic Newton step}

Because \(G^{(4)}\) is homogeneous of degree \(3\),
Euler's theorem gives
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
J^{(4)}(a)\,a = 3\,G^{(4)}(a).
}
\label{eq:euler_cubic}
\end{equation}

Therefore, at the corrected seed,
\begin{equation}
J_f^{(4)}\,\delta_f = 3\,G^{(4)}(\delta_f).
\end{equation}

If one forms the quartic-only Newton correction
\begin{equation}
\Delta_f^{(4)}
:=
-\bigl(J_f^{(4)}\bigr)^{-1}G^{(4)}(\delta_f),
\end{equation}
then \eqref{eq:euler_cubic} implies immediately
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
\Delta_f^{(4)}=-\frac13\,\delta_f.
}
\end{equation}

Hence
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
\Delta_f^{(4)}
=
(-0.0032,\ 0.0096,\ -0.0032).
}
\end{equation}

This is an exact local certificate of the quartic model, not a numerical accident.

\paragraph{Meaning.}
If the full extracted Jacobian \(J_f\) and full residual vector \(G_f:=G(\delta_f)\) were governed primarily by the coarse quartic closure, then the full local Newton correction
\[
\Delta_f:=-J_f^{-1}G_f
\]
should lie close to
\[
-\delta_f/3.
\]

Thus the deviation
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
\mathfrak D_{\rm Newt}
:=
\left\|
\Delta_f+\frac13\delta_f
\right\|
}
\end{equation}
is a sharp quantitative measure of the non-quartic local structure.

\section{4. Full local Taylor model}

Write the full corrected residual map as
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
G(\delta_f+\Delta)
=
G_f
+
J_f\Delta
+
\frac12\,\mathcal H_f[\Delta,\Delta]
+
O(\|\Delta\|^3),
}
\end{equation}
where
\[
G_f:=G(\delta_f),\qquad
J_f:=DG(\delta_f).
\]

Split
\begin{equation}
G=G^{(2)}+G^{(4)}+G^{(\ge5)}.
\end{equation}
Then
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
J_f
=
J_f^{(2)}
+
J_f^{(4)}
+
J_f^{(\ge5)}.
}
\end{equation}

Since \(J_f^{(4)}\) is already known exactly, the real unknown part is
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
J_f^{\rm rem}:=J_f-J_f^{(4)}=J_f^{(2)}+J_f^{(\ge5)}.
}
\end{equation}

So the next finite audit is to extract \(J_f\) and then inspect \(J_f^{\rm rem}\).

\section{5. Coordinate-basis centered-difference extraction}

Let
\[
e_A=(1,0,0),\qquad
e_E=(0,1,0),\qquad
e_O=(0,0,1).
\]
For an admissible step \(\varepsilon>0\), define the centered-difference estimate
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
J_{\cdot Y}^{[\varepsilon]}
=
\frac{
G(\delta_f+\varepsilon e_Y)-G(\delta_f-\varepsilon e_Y)
}{
2\varepsilon
},
\qquad
Y\in\{A,E,O\}.
}
\label{eq:coord_fd}
\end{equation}

This uses six residual evaluations:
\[
\delta_f\pm\varepsilon e_A,\qquad
\delta_f\pm\varepsilon e_E,\qquad
\delta_f\pm\varepsilon e_O.
\]

The truncation error is
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
J^{[\varepsilon]}=J_f+O(\varepsilon^2).
}
\end{equation}

A Richardson-improved estimate is
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
J^{\rm Rich}
=
\frac{4J^{[\varepsilon/2]}-J^{[\varepsilon]}}{3}
=
J_f+O(\varepsilon^4),
}
\end{equation}
which requires twelve evaluations rather than six.

\section{6. Adapted-basis extraction}

Because the corrected local valley is narrow and organized around the sign pattern
\[
(+,-,+),
\]
it is more honest to use the orthonormal adapted basis
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
t=\frac{1}{\sqrt{11}}(1,-3,1),\qquad
n_1=\frac{1}{\sqrt2}(1,0,-1),\qquad
n_2=\frac{1}{\sqrt{22}}(3,2,3).
}
\end{equation}

Here \(t\) is the corrected-branch tangent direction, while \(n_1,n_2\) span the transverse plane.

Define
\[
B=(t\,|\,n_1\,|\,n_2)\in O(3).
\]

Then the adapted finite-difference columns are
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
\widetilde J_{\cdot k}^{[\varepsilon]}
=
\frac{
G(\delta_f+\varepsilon b_k)-G(\delta_f-\varepsilon b_k)
}{
2\varepsilon
},
\qquad
b_1=t,\ b_2=n_1,\ b_3=n_2.
}
\label{eq:adapted_fd}
\end{equation}

Since \(B\) is orthogonal, the coordinate-basis Jacobian is reconstructed by
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
J_f \approx \widetilde J\,B^T.
}
\end{equation}

\paragraph{Why this basis matters.}
The adapted stencil separates:

\begin{enumerate}
\item the along-branch linear response \(J_ft\),
\item the first transverse symmetry-breaking response \(J_fn_1\),
\item the second transverse symmetry-breaking response \(J_fn_2\).
\end{enumerate}

This is the correct local certificate if the admissible corrected valley is truly narrow.

\section{7. Exact quartic Jacobian in the adapted basis}

Transform the exact quartic Jacobian:
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
\widetilde J_f^{(4)}:=B^T J_f^{(4)} B.
}
\end{equation}

One obtains
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
\widetilde J_f^{(4)}
=
\begin{pmatrix}
23.24934224 & 3.78278610 & -3.12859268\\
3.78278610 & 6.43553954 & 1.80527992\\
-3.12859268 & 1.80527992 & 6.66026178
\end{pmatrix}.
}
\end{equation}

Thus the quartic local stiffness is dominated along the corrected-branch tangent \(t\),
while the two transverse directions remain softer but still positive.

This gives a physically meaningful comparison template for the extracted full Jacobian:
\[
\widetilde J_f-\widetilde J_f^{(4)}.
\]

\section{8. Admissibility protocol for the step size}

The finite-difference stencil must remain inside the geometry-preserving corrected local region.
A practical admissibility rule is:

\begin{equation}
\boxed{
\text{accept }\delta
\quad\text{only if}\quad
\texttt{shell\_fraction}(\delta)\ge s_\star,
\qquad
\texttt{orbit\_balance}(\delta)\ge b_\star,
}
\end{equation}
with conservative thresholds chosen slightly below the corrected-seed values.
For example, one may start with
\[
s_\star=0.90,\qquad b_\star=0.88.
\]

Then perform backtracking:
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
\varepsilon\mapsto \varepsilon/2
\quad\text{until all stencil points are admissible.}
}
\end{equation}

This is required because the residual-based reduced object is only meaningful within the geometry-preserving local window.

\section{9. Local certificate quantities}

Once \(J_f\) is extracted, the minimal local certificate should report the following finite quantities:

\paragraph{(i) Jacobian remainder}
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
\mathfrak D_J
:=
\frac{\|J_f-J_f^{(4)}\|_F}{\|J_f^{(4)}\|_F}.
}
\end{equation}

\paragraph{(ii) Newton deviation}
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
\mathfrak D_{\rm Newt}
:=
\frac{
\left\|
-J_f^{-1}G_f+\delta_f/3
\right\|
}{
\|\delta_f\|
}.
}
\end{equation}

\paragraph{(iii) Tangent/transverse mismatch in adapted basis}
Let
\[
\widetilde J_f=B^T J_f B.
\]
Then define
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
\mathfrak D_t
:=
\frac{|\widetilde J_{11}-\widetilde J_{11}^{(4)}|}{|\widetilde J_{11}^{(4)}|},
\qquad
\mathfrak D_\perp
:=
\frac{
\left\|
\widetilde J_{(2,3)\times(2,3)}-\widetilde J_{(2,3)\times(2,3)}^{(4)}
\right\|_F
}{
\left\|
\widetilde J_{(2,3)\times(2,3)}^{(4)}
\right\|_F
}.
}
\end{equation}

These are the correct certificate-level diagnostics of whether the coarse four-class quartic closure is locally sufficient.

\section{10. Honest pass/fail logic}

The next local decision should be made as follows.

\paragraph{Pass (coarse four-class locally sufficient).}
If
\[
\mathfrak D_J\ll1,\qquad
\mathfrak D_{\rm Newt}\ll1,
\]
and the adapted tangent/transverse mismatches are also small, then the corrected local branch is already largely controlled by the coarse four-class quartic closure.

\paragraph{Fail (immediate refinement required).}
If either
\[
\mathfrak D_J=O(1)
\quad\text{or}\quad
\mathfrak D_{\rm Newt}=O(1),
\]
then the unresolved local structure is not a small perturbation.
In that case one must move immediately to one of:

\begin{enumerate}
\item a nontrivial quadratic Jacobian normalization layer,
\item higher reduced residual terms \(G^{(\ge5)}\),
\item sign-resolved suborbit quartic refinement.
\end{enumerate}

\section{Final summary}

The exact quartic reduced residual Jacobian at the corrected seed is already known:
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
J_f^{(4)}
=
\begin{pmatrix}
10.095240102467715 & -4.991665534361308 & 0.41377801570768584\\
-4.991665534361308 & 22.64650843997611 & -1.2413340471230576\\
0.41377801570768584 & -1.2413340471230576 & 3.6033950146440785
\end{pmatrix}.
}
\end{equation}

Its eigenvalues are all positive, and its exact quartic-only Newton correction is
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
\Delta_f^{(4)}=-\frac13\delta_f=(-0.0032,0.0096,-0.0032).
}
\end{equation}

Therefore the remaining unknown local structure is completely captured by the finite audit object
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
J_f-J_f^{(4)},
}
\end{equation}
where
\[
J_f
=
\left.
\frac{\partial G}{\partial \delta}
\right|_{\delta=\delta_f}.
\]

So the next executable task is finite and explicit:
\[
\boxed{
\text{extract }J_f\text{ by six- or twelve-point centered differences and compare it directly to }J_f^{(4)}.
}
\]

\section{Final summary}

\begin{equation}
\boxed{
\text{Current status: the correct next local object is the full residual Jacobian }J_f=DG(\delta_f).
}
\end{equation}

The corrected seed is frozen at
\[
\delta_f=(0.0096,-0.0288,0.0096),
\]
and the reduced object is the projected residual map
\[
G(\delta)=\bigl(G_A(\delta),G_E(\delta),G_O(\delta)\bigr)^T.
\]

The exact quartic local linearization is already known:
\[
J_f^{(4)}
=
\begin{pmatrix}
10.095240102467715 & -4.991665534361308 & 0.41377801570768584\\
-4.991665534361308 & 22.64650843997611 & -1.2413340471230576\\
0.41377801570768584 & -1.2413340471230576 & 3.6033950146440785
\end{pmatrix}.
\]

It is positive definite, with eigenvalues
\[
3.5212959,\qquad 8.35219818,\qquad 24.47164948.
\]

Because the quartic residual is homogeneous of degree \(3\),
\[
J_f^{(4)}\delta_f=3G^{(4)}(\delta_f),
\]
so the exact quartic-only Newton correction is
\[
\boxed{
\Delta_f^{(4)}=-(J_f^{(4)})^{-1}G^{(4)}(\delta_f)=-\delta_f/3.
}
\]

Hence the finite local certificate should compare the extracted full Jacobian \(J_f\) to \(J_f^{(4)}\) using
\[
\mathfrak D_J
=
\frac{\|J_f-J_f^{(4)}\|_F}{\|J_f^{(4)}\|_F},
\qquad
\mathfrak D_{\rm Newt}
=
\frac{\|-J_f^{-1}G_f+\delta_f/3\|}{\|\delta_f\|}.
\]

If these are small, the coarse four-class quartic closure is locally sufficient.
If they are order one, then the unresolved local structure is not a small perturbation and one must immediately refine the reduced model.

\begin{equation}
\boxed{
\text{Goal C: extract the full local Jacobian }J_f\text{ at the corrected seed.}
}
\end{equation}
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
\text{Goal M: compare }J_f\text{ against the exact quartic baseline }J_f^{(4)}.
}
\end{equation}
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
\text{Goal S: decide from }J_f-J_f^{(4)}\text{ whether coarse four-class closure is locally sufficient or whether a higher reduced refinement is unavoidable.}
}
\end{equation}

%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%

\section{Post-Jacobian Decision Protocol: From \(J_f\) to the Next Exact Refinement}

\section{Short explanation}

The next executable object is the local residual Jacobian
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
J_f:=DG(\delta_f),
\qquad
\delta_f=(0.0096,-0.0288,0.0096).
}
\end{equation}

But extracting \(J_f\) is not yet the end of the local audit.
The true next decision is controlled by the remainder
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
J_f^{\rm rem}:=J_f-J_f^{(4)},
}
\end{equation}
where the exact quartic baseline is already known:
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
J_f^{(4)}
=
\begin{pmatrix}
10.095240102467715 & -4.991665534361308 & 0.41377801570768584\\
-4.991665534361308 & 22.64650843997611 & -1.2413340471230576\\
0.41377801570768584 & -1.2413340471230576 & 3.6033950146440785
\end{pmatrix}.
}
\end{equation}

So the next exact question is:

\begin{equation}
\boxed{
\text{Is }J_f^{\rm rem}\text{ small, diagonal-dominant, full-matrix but coarse, or genuinely beyond the coarse }(A,E,O)\text{ basis?}
}
\end{equation}

\section{1. Exact quartic baseline recap}

The quartic baseline is positive definite with eigenvalues
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
\lambda_1^{(4)}\approx 3.5212959,\qquad
\lambda_2^{(4)}\approx 8.35219818,\qquad
\lambda_3^{(4)}\approx 24.47164948.
}
\end{equation}

Its Frobenius norm is
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
\|J_f^{(4)}\|_F
\approx
26.09636693908683.
}
\end{equation}

Because the quartic residual is cubic-homogeneous, the exact quartic-only Newton step is
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
\Delta_f^{(4)}
=
-\bigl(J_f^{(4)}\bigr)^{-1}G^{(4)}(\delta_f)
=
-\frac13\,\delta_f
=
(-0.0032,\ 0.0096,\ -0.0032).
}
\end{equation}

Therefore the full local deviation from quartic control is measured by how much the extracted full Jacobian \(J_f\) and the actual Newton step differ from this exact quartic prediction.

\section{2. Finite extraction of the full Jacobian}

Let \(b_1,b_2,b_3\) be either the coordinate basis
\[
e_A,\ e_E,\ e_O
\]
or the adapted basis
\[
t=\frac{1}{\sqrt{11}}(1,-3,1),
\qquad
n_1=\frac{1}{\sqrt2}(1,0,-1),
\qquad
n_2=\frac{1}{\sqrt{22}}(3,2,3).
\]

For an admissible step \(\varepsilon>0\), define the centered-difference estimate
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
\widetilde J_{\cdot k}^{[\varepsilon]}
=
\frac{
G(\delta_f+\varepsilon b_k)-G(\delta_f-\varepsilon b_k)
}{
2\varepsilon
},
\qquad
k=1,2,3.
}
\end{equation}

If the basis is the adapted one and
\[
B=(b_1\,|\,b_2\,|\,b_3)\in O(3),
\]
then
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
J_f\approx \widetilde J^{[\varepsilon]} B^T.
}
\end{equation}

A Richardson-upgraded estimate is
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
\widetilde J^{\rm Rich}
=
\frac{4\widetilde J^{[\varepsilon/2]}-\widetilde J^{[\varepsilon]}}{3},
}
\end{equation}
with
\[
\widetilde J^{\rm Rich}
=
\widetilde J_f+O(\varepsilon^4).
\]

\section{3. First decomposition of the remainder}

Once \(J_f\) is extracted, define the remainder
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
J_f^{\rm rem}:=J_f-J_f^{(4)}.
}
\end{equation}

Now split it into symmetric and antisymmetric parts:
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
S_f:=\frac12\left(J_f^{\rm rem}+(J_f^{\rm rem})^T\right),
\qquad
A_f:=\frac12\left(J_f^{\rm rem}-(J_f^{\rm rem})^T\right).
}
\end{equation}

This is the first sharp local test.

\paragraph{Interpretation.}
\begin{itemize}
\item \(S_f\) is the true additional local linear stiffness / instability layer.
\item \(A_f\) measures local non-gradient behavior of the residual map in the chosen reduced coordinates.
\end{itemize}

If \(A_f\) is numerically negligible, then the local reduced residual map is approximately gradient-like.
If \(A_f\) is not negligible, then a naive local effective free-energy interpretation is still invalid even at Jacobian level.

Define the antisymmetry diagnostic
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
\mathfrak D_{\rm skew}
:=
\frac{\|A_f\|_F}{\|J_f\|_F}.
}
\end{equation}

\section{4. Best diagonal coarse quadratic fit}

The minimal coarse quadratic correction model is diagonal:
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
J_f \approx J_f^{(4)} + D_f,
\qquad
D_f=\operatorname{diag}(d_A,d_E,d_O).
}
\end{equation}

The Frobenius-best diagonal fit is simply
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
D_f^{\star}:=\operatorname{diag}\bigl((J_f^{\rm rem})_{AA},(J_f^{\rm rem})_{EE},(J_f^{\rm rem})_{OO}\bigr).
}
\end{equation}

The corresponding diagonal-fit defect is
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
\mathfrak D_{\rm diag}
:=
\frac{\|J_f^{\rm rem}-D_f^{\star}\|_F}{\|J_f^{\rm rem}\|_F}.
}
\end{equation}

\paragraph{Meaning.}
\begin{itemize}
\item If \(\mathfrak D_{\rm diag}\ll1\), then the next correction beyond the quartic baseline is essentially an orbitwise quadratic layer.
\item If \(\mathfrak D_{\rm diag}=O(1)\), then a diagonal quadratic correction is not enough.
\end{itemize}

\section{5. Best symmetric coarse quadratic fit}

If diagonal fitting fails, the next minimal model is a full symmetric \(3\times3\) coarse matrix:
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
J_f \approx J_f^{(4)} + K_f,
\qquad
K_f=K_f^T.
}
\end{equation}

The Frobenius-best symmetric fit is
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
K_f^\star:=S_f.
}
\end{equation}

Then the remaining non-symmetric defect is exactly \(A_f\), and the symmetric-coarse-fit defect is
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
\mathfrak D_{\rm sym}
:=
\frac{\|A_f\|_F}{\|J_f^{\rm rem}\|_F}.
}
\end{equation}

If \(\mathfrak D_{\rm sym}\ll1\) but \(\mathfrak D_{\rm diag}=O(1)\), then the corrected local branch is still describable at the coarse \((A,E,O)\) level, but only through a full symmetric quadratic matrix, not an orbitwise scalar correction.

\section{6. Adapted-basis branch decomposition}

Now transform to the adapted basis
\[
B=(t\,|\,n_1\,|\,n_2).
\]
Define
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
\widetilde J_f:=B^T J_f B,
\qquad
\widetilde J_f^{(4)}:=B^T J_f^{(4)} B,
\qquad
\widetilde J_f^{\rm rem}:=\widetilde J_f-\widetilde J_f^{(4)}.
}
\end{equation}

Then write
\[
\widetilde J_f^{\rm rem}
=
\begin{pmatrix}
r_{tt} & r_{t\perp_1} & r_{t\perp_2}\\
r_{\perp_1 t} & r_{\perp_1\perp_1} & r_{\perp_1\perp_2}\\
r_{\perp_2 t} & r_{\perp_2\perp_1} & r_{\perp_2\perp_2}
\end{pmatrix}.
\]

This gives the physically sharp local decomposition:
\begin{itemize}
\item \(r_{tt}\): additional along-branch stiffness,
\item \(r_{t\perp_i}\): tangent/transverse mixing,
\item \(r_{\perp_i\perp_j}\): transverse residual structure.
\end{itemize}

Define
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
\mathfrak D_{t\leftrightarrow\perp}
:=
\frac{
\sqrt{|r_{t\perp_1}|^2+|r_{t\perp_2}|^2+|r_{\perp_1 t}|^2+|r_{\perp_2 t}|^2}
}{
\|\widetilde J_f^{\rm rem}\|_F
},
}
\end{equation}
and
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
\mathfrak D_{\perp\text{-}blk}
:=
\frac{
\left\|
\begin{pmatrix}
r_{\perp_1\perp_1} & r_{\perp_1\perp_2}\\
r_{\perp_2\perp_1} & r_{\perp_2\perp_2}
\end{pmatrix}
\right\|_F
}{
\|\widetilde J_f^{\rm rem}\|_F
}.
}
\end{equation}

\paragraph{Meaning.}
\begin{itemize}
\item If \(\mathfrak D_{t\leftrightarrow\perp}\ll1\), the corrected valley decouples cleanly at linear order.
\item If \(\mathfrak D_{t\leftrightarrow\perp}=O(1)\), the coarse corrected branch is not a clean one-dimensional valley even locally.
\end{itemize}

\section{7. Newton-level decision}

Let
\[
G_f:=G(\delta_f),
\qquad
\Delta_f:=-J_f^{-1}G_f.
\]
The quartic baseline predicts
\[
\Delta_f^{(4)}=-\delta_f/3.
\]

Define the Newton mismatch
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
\mathfrak D_{\rm Newt}
:=
\frac{\left\|\Delta_f+\delta_f/3\right\|}{\|\delta_f\|}.
}
\end{equation}

Then:

\begin{itemize}
\item If \(\mathfrak D_{\rm Newt}\ll1\), the local corrected branch is quartic-dominated.
\item If \(\mathfrak D_{\rm Newt}=O(1)\), then the non-quartic local structure is not perturbative.
\end{itemize}

\section{8. Exact local decision tree}

The next-step decision can now be made without ambiguity.

\paragraph{Case I: quartic-local sufficiency}
If
\[
\mathfrak D_J
=
\frac{\|J_f-J_f^{(4)}\|_F}{\|J_f^{(4)}\|_F}
\ll1
\]
and
\[
\mathfrak D_{\rm Newt}\ll1,
\]
then the coarse four-class quartic closure is locally sufficient.
No further reduced refinement is needed yet.

\paragraph{Case II: diagonal quadratic correction sufficient}
If \(\mathfrak D_J\) is not small but
\[
\mathfrak D_{\rm diag}\ll1,
\qquad
\mathfrak D_{\rm skew}\ll1,
\]
then the next correct reduced model is
\[
J_f\approx J_f^{(4)}+D_f^\star.
\]
That is, one adds an orbitwise quadratic normalization layer and stays in the coarse \((A,E,O)\) basis.

\paragraph{Case III: full symmetric coarse correction required}
If
\[
\mathfrak D_{\rm diag}=O(1),
\qquad
\mathfrak D_{\rm sym}\ll1,
\]
then the correct next reduced model is
\[
J_f\approx J_f^{(4)}+K_f^\star,
\qquad K_f^\star=S_f,
\]
namely a full symmetric coarse \(3\times3\) quadratic matrix.

\paragraph{Case IV: residual map remains genuinely non-gradient}
If
\[
\mathfrak D_{\rm skew}=O(1),
\]
then even the corrected local reduced map is not approximately gradient-like.
In that case one must keep the projected residual system itself as primary and abandon any local reduced free-energy interpretation at this level.

\paragraph{Case V: coarse orbit basis itself fails}
If
\[
\mathfrak D_{t\leftrightarrow\perp}=O(1)
\quad\text{or}\quad
\mathfrak D_{\perp\text{-}blk}=O(1)
\]
in a way inconsistent with the coarse \((A,E,O)\) truncation, then the next unavoidable refinement is the sign-resolved suborbit basis
\[
(A_+,A_-,E_+,E_-,O_{++},O_{+-},O_{-+},O_{--}).
\]

\section{9. Minimal executable pseudocode logic}

The full audit logic is:

\begin{equation}
\boxed{
\delta_f
\to
\{G(\delta_f\pm\varepsilon b_k)\}_{k=1}^3
\to
J_f
\to
J_f^{\rm rem}=J_f-J_f^{(4)}
\to
(S_f,A_f,D_f^\star,\widetilde J_f^{\rm rem},\Delta_f)
\to
\text{decision tree}.
}
\end{equation}

Thus the next local frontier is no longer conceptual.
It is a finite matrix audit with a unique pass/fail/refine logic.

\section{Final summary}

Once the full local Jacobian \(J_f\) is extracted, the only honest remaining question is how to classify
\[
J_f^{\rm rem}:=J_f-J_f^{(4)}.
\]

This classification is exact and finite:

\begin{itemize}
\item compare \(J_f\) to the quartic baseline \(J_f^{(4)}\),
\item test diagonal versus full symmetric versus non-gradient remainder structure,
\item inspect tangent/transverse mixing in the adapted basis,
\item compare the full Newton step to the exact quartic prediction \(-\delta_f/3\).
\end{itemize}

This completely determines the next mathematically correct move:
stay with coarse four-class closure, add a diagonal quadratic layer, add a full symmetric coarse quadratic layer, or jump to a sign-resolved suborbit refinement.

\section{Final summary}

\begin{equation}
\boxed{
\text{Current status: after extracting }J_f,\text{ the true local unknown is the remainder }J_f^{\rm rem}=J_f-J_f^{(4)}.
}
\end{equation}

The corrected branch is frozen at
\[
(\mu^2,\lambda,\gamma)=(0.26,-0.43,1.62),
\qquad
\delta_f=(0.0096,-0.0288,0.0096),
\]
and the correct reduced object is the projected residual map
\[
G(\delta)=\bigl(G_A(\delta),G_E(\delta),G_O(\delta)\bigr)^T.
\]

The exact quartic local baseline is already known:
\[
J_f^{(4)}
=
\begin{pmatrix}
10.095240102467715 & -4.991665534361308 & 0.41377801570768584\\
-4.991665534361308 & 22.64650843997611 & -1.2413340471230576\\
0.41377801570768584 & -1.2413340471230576 & 3.6033950146440785
\end{pmatrix},
\]
with exact quartic Newton correction
\[
\Delta_f^{(4)}=-\delta_f/3.
\]

Therefore the next local audit is finite and unambiguous:
\[
\boxed{
J_f
\to
J_f^{\rm rem}
\to
(S_f,A_f,D_f^\star,\widetilde J_f^{\rm rem},\Delta_f)
\to
\text{decision}.
}
\]

The decision logic is:

\begin{enumerate}
\item If \(J_f-J_f^{(4)}\) is small and the Newton mismatch is small, the coarse four-class quartic closure is locally sufficient.
\item If the remainder is not small but is approximately diagonal and symmetric, add an orbitwise quadratic normalization layer.
\item If the remainder is symmetric but not diagonal, add a full symmetric coarse \(3\times3\) quadratic layer.
\item If the remainder has a large antisymmetric part, keep the projected residual map as primary and do not reintroduce a local free-energy interpretation.
\item If tangent/transverse mixing is large in the adapted basis, the coarse \((A,E,O)\) basis itself fails and one must move to the sign-resolved suborbit basis.
\end{enumerate}

\begin{equation}
\boxed{
\text{Goal C: extract }J_f\text{ and classify }J_f-J_f^{(4)}.
}
\end{equation}
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
\text{Goal M: determine whether the next correction is diagonal, full symmetric coarse, or genuinely non-gradient.}
}
\end{equation}
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
\text{Goal S: decide whether the coarse }(A,E,O)\text{ basis still survives locally or whether sign-resolved suborbit refinement is unavoidable.}
}
\end{equation}

\section{Quantitative Remainder Theorem Around the Corrected Seed}

\section{Setup}

The corrected residual stage is frozen at
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
(\mu^2,\lambda,\gamma)=(0.26,-0.43,1.62),
\qquad
\delta_f=(0.0096,-0.0288,0.0096).
}
\end{equation}

The correct reduced object is the projected residual map
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
G(\delta)
=
\begin{pmatrix}
G_A(\delta)\\
G_E(\delta)\\
G_O(\delta)
\end{pmatrix},
}
\end{equation}
and the corrected diagnostics give
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
\|G(\delta_f)\|
\approx
0.03791852545979427.
}
\end{equation}

The corrected extractor outputs
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
\sigma_0=-109.41971030446824,
\qquad
\eta=-6.502583100163014,
}
\end{equation}
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
\sigma_A=-115.81015796064617,
\quad
\sigma_E=-105.83201242243113,
\quad
\sigma_O=-108.719022860933,
}
\end{equation}
and
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
U_d=2493.1087739398513,
\quad
U_{\rm opp}=14874.171297437068,
\quad
U_\perp=9027.17290917827,
\quad
U_{\rm obl}=2244.8894311890813.
}
\end{equation}

These quantities must be interpreted honestly as residual-based effective reduced coefficients of the current solver family, not yet theorem-level coefficients of a fully closed microscopic energy functional.

\section{1. Exact quartic baseline objects}

From the coarse four-class closure, the quartic residual vector at the corrected seed is
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
G_f^{(4)}
=
\begin{pmatrix}
0.08154884710518008\\
-0.23735207970690309\\
0.024771760656218685
\end{pmatrix},
\qquad
\|G_f^{(4)}\|
\approx
0.2521901352777009.
}
\end{equation}

The exact quartic Jacobian at the corrected seed is
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
J_f^{(4)}
=
\begin{pmatrix}
10.095240102467715 & -4.991665534361308 & 0.41377801570768584\\
-4.991665534361308 & 22.64650843997611 & -1.2413340471230576\\
0.41377801570768584 & -1.2413340471230576 & 3.6033950146440785
\end{pmatrix}.
}
\end{equation}

Its smallest eigenvalue is
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
\lambda_*:=\lambda_{\min}(J_f^{(4)})\approx 3.5212959.
}
\end{equation}

Also,
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
\|\delta_f\|
=
\sqrt{(0.0096)^2+(-0.0288)^2+(0.0096)^2}
\approx
0.03183959798741184.
}
\end{equation}

The quartic-only Newton correction is exactly
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
\Delta_f^{(4)}
=
-\bigl(J_f^{(4)}\bigr)^{-1}G_f^{(4)}
=
-\frac13\,\delta_f
=
(-0.0032,\,0.0096,\,-0.0032),
}
\end{equation}
so
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
\|\Delta_f^{(4)}\|
=
\frac13\|\delta_f\|
\approx
0.01061319932913728.
}
\end{equation}

\section{2. Exact decomposition of the unknown local structure}

Let the full local Jacobian and full residual vector be
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
J_f:=DG(\delta_f),
\qquad
G_f:=G(\delta_f).
}
\end{equation}

Define the remainders
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
R_f:=J_f-J_f^{(4)},
\qquad
E_f:=G_f-G_f^{(4)}.
}
\end{equation}

Then
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
J_f=J_f^{(4)}+R_f,
\qquad
G_f=G_f^{(4)}+E_f.
}
\end{equation}

This is the exact local split.

\section{3. Strong nonquartic forcing already visible at vector level}

We know only the norm of the full corrected residual vector:
\[
\|G_f\|\approx 0.03791852545979427.
\]
Therefore the triangle inequality yields
\begin{equation}
\bigl|\|G_f^{(4)}\|-\|G_f\|\bigr|
\le
\|E_f\|
\le
\|G_f^{(4)}\|+\|G_f\|.
\end{equation}

Substituting the known norms gives
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
0.21427160981790663
\le
\|E_f\|
\le
0.2901086607374952.
}
\end{equation}

Dividing by \(\|G_f^{(4)}\|\approx 0.2521901352777009\), one gets
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
0.8496431059127669
\le
\frac{\|E_f\|}{\|G_f^{(4)}\|}
\le
1.1503568940872333.
}
\end{equation}

\begin{theorem}[Strong nonquartic forcing at the corrected seed]
The nonquartic residual vector \(E_f=G_f-G_f^{(4)}\) is necessarily of order one relative to the quartic baseline:
\[
\frac{\|E_f\|}{\|G_f^{(4)}\|}\ge 0.8496431059127669.
\]
Hence quartic-only local sufficiency is already excluded at the residual-vector level.
\end{theorem}

\begin{proof}
Immediate from the reverse triangle inequality.
\end{proof}

\paragraph{Interpretation.}
This does \emph{not} yet prove that the Jacobian remainder \(R_f\) is large.
But it proves that the full corrected residual vector is obtained only after a substantial cancellation against the quartic baseline.
Therefore the next local question is not whether nonquartic structure exists, but what matrix type it has.

\section{4. Remainder theorem for invertibility}

Assume
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
\|R_f\|_2<\lambda_*.
}
\end{equation}
Then \(J_f\) is invertible, because
\[
J_f=J_f^{(4)}\bigl(I+(J_f^{(4)})^{-1}R_f\bigr),
\]
and
\[
\|(J_f^{(4)})^{-1}R_f\|_2
\le
\|(J_f^{(4)})^{-1}\|_2\,\|R_f\|_2
=
\frac{\|R_f\|_2}{\lambda_*}
<1.
\]

Therefore the Neumann series converges, and
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
\|J_f^{-1}\|_2
\le
\frac{1}{\lambda_*-\|R_f\|_2}.
}
\end{equation}

\begin{theorem}[Quantitative invertibility criterion]
If
\[
\|R_f\|_2<\lambda_*\approx 3.5212959,
\]
then the full local Jacobian \(J_f\) is invertible and satisfies
\[
\|J_f^{-1}\|_2
\le
\frac{1}{3.5212959-\|R_f\|_2}.
\]
\end{theorem}

\section{5. Exact Newton-step remainder identity}

Let
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
\Delta_f:=-J_f^{-1}G_f
}
\end{equation}
be the full local Newton correction.

Using
\[
J_f=J_f^{(4)}+R_f,
\qquad
G_f=G_f^{(4)}+E_f,
\qquad
J_f^{(4)}\Delta_f^{(4)}=-G_f^{(4)},
\]
we get
\begin{align}
\Delta_f
&=
-(J_f^{(4)}+R_f)^{-1}(G_f^{(4)}+E_f)
\\
&=
\Delta_f^{(4)}
-
J_f^{-1}\bigl(R_f\Delta_f^{(4)}+E_f\bigr).
\end{align}

Hence the exact difference formula is
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
\Delta_f-\Delta_f^{(4)}
=
-
J_f^{-1}\bigl(R_f\Delta_f^{(4)}+E_f\bigr).
}
\label{eq:newton_exact_remainder}
\end{equation}

Taking norms and using the invertibility bound above gives
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
\|\Delta_f-\Delta_f^{(4)}\|
\le
\frac{
\|R_f\|_2\,\|\Delta_f^{(4)}\|
+
\|E_f\|
}{
\lambda_*-\|R_f\|_2
}.
}
\end{equation}

Since
\[
\|\Delta_f^{(4)}\|=\|\delta_f\|/3,
\]
this becomes
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
\|\Delta_f+\delta_f/3\|
\le
\frac{
\|E_f\|
+
(\|R_f\|_2/3)\,\|\delta_f\|
}{
\lambda_*-\|R_f\|_2
}.
}
\end{equation}

Dividing by \(\|\delta_f\|\), the normalized Newton mismatch obeys
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
\mathfrak D_{\rm Newt}
:=
\frac{\|\Delta_f+\delta_f/3\|}{\|\delta_f\|}
\le
\frac{
\|E_f\|/\|\delta_f\|+\|R_f\|_2/3
}{
\lambda_*-\|R_f\|_2
}.
}
\label{eq:newton_mismatch_bound}
\end{equation}

\section{6. Consequence of the known norm data}

Because \(\|E_f\|\ge 0.21427160981790663\) and
\[
\|\delta_f\|\approx 0.03183959798741184,
\]
the forcing term in \eqref{eq:newton_mismatch_bound} satisfies
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
\frac{\|E_f\|}{\|\delta_f\|}
\ge
6.729308620179073.
}
\end{equation}

So even before \(J_f\) is extracted, the full Newton step can only be close to the quartic predictor \(-\delta_f/3\) if the inverse \(J_f^{-1}\) and the vector \(R_f\Delta_f^{(4)}+E_f\) produce a substantial directional cancellation.

In particular:

\begin{equation}
\boxed{
\text{the strong form of quartic-local sufficiency is already ruled out.}
}
\end{equation}

What remains open is the weaker question:
\[
\boxed{
\text{is }R_f\text{ small and structured, or is the local nonquartic sector intrinsically matrix-large?}
}
\]

\section{7. Quantitative decision parameters after extracting \texorpdfstring{$J_f$}{Jf}}

Once \(J_f\) is extracted, define
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
\mathfrak D_J^{(2)}
:=
\frac{\|R_f\|_2}{\lambda_*},
\qquad
\mathfrak D_J^{(F)}
:=
\frac{\|R_f\|_F}{\|J_f^{(4)}\|_F}.
}
\end{equation}

The mathematically sharp thresholds are:

\begin{equation}
\boxed{
\mathfrak D_J^{(2)}<1
\Longrightarrow
J_f\ \text{is certainly invertible}.
}
\end{equation}

A useful stronger perturbative regime is
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
\mathfrak D_J^{(2)}\le 0.2
\quad\Longleftrightarrow\quad
\|R_f\|_2\le 0.70425918,
}
\end{equation}
and a moderate one is
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
\mathfrak D_J^{(2)}\le 0.5
\quad\Longleftrightarrow\quad
\|R_f\|_2\le 1.76064795.
}
\end{equation}

If \(\mathfrak D_J^{(2)}\) is already \(O(1)\), then the quartic baseline is not the dominant local Jacobian.

\section{8. What the next extraction will actually decide}

The next finite audit,
\[
J_f
\quad\Longrightarrow\quad
R_f=J_f-J_f^{(4)},
\]
will decide between the following logically distinct possibilities:

\begin{enumerate}
\item \(R_f\) is small:
the quartic baseline controls the local Jacobian, but not the residual vector.
\item \(R_f\) is not small but approximately diagonal:
the next correct reduced model is a diagonal coarse quadratic normalization layer.
\item \(R_f\) is symmetric but not diagonal:
the next correct reduced model is a full symmetric coarse \(3\times 3\) quadratic layer.
\item \(R_f\) has a large antisymmetric part:
the local reduced residual map is genuinely non-gradient even at Jacobian level.
\item \(R_f\) destroys the tangent/transverse split in the adapted basis:
the coarse \((A,E,O)\) reduction itself fails locally and sign-resolved suborbit refinement is unavoidable.
\end{enumerate}

\section{Final summary}

The corrected residual vector already proves a strong fact:
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
\|G_f-G_f^{(4)}\|
\ge
0.21427160981790663,
}
\end{equation}
hence
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
\frac{\|G_f-G_f^{(4)}\|}{\|G_f^{(4)}\|}
\ge
0.8496431059127669.
}
\end{equation}

So quartic-only local sufficiency is already excluded at vector level.

The only remaining local unknown is the matrix remainder
\[
R_f=J_f-J_f^{(4)}.
\]
If
\[
\|R_f\|_2<3.5212959,
\]
then \(J_f\) is automatically invertible. Moreover the Newton-step deviation satisfies
\[
\boxed{
\|\Delta_f+\delta_f/3\|
\le
\frac{
\|E_f\|+(\|R_f\|_2/3)\|\delta_f\|
}{
3.5212959-\|R_f\|_2
}.
}
\]

Therefore the next exact task is uniquely fixed:
\[
\boxed{
\text{extract }J_f,\text{ compute }R_f=J_f-J_f^{(4)},\text{ and classify }R_f.
}
\]

\section{Final summary}

\begin{equation}
\boxed{
\text{Current status: quartic-local sufficiency is already excluded in the strong vector-level sense.}

The corrected seed and residual map are fixed as

\section{Quantitative No-Go for Strong Quartic-Local Sufficiency}

\section{Setup}

The corrected residual stage is frozen at

with corrected residual norm

\section{1. Exact quartic baseline objects}

The coarse four-class quartic closure gives
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
G_f^{(4)}
=
\begin{pmatrix}
0.08154884710518008\\
-0.23735207970690309\\
0.024771760656218685
\end{pmatrix},
\qquad
\|G_f^{(4)}\|
\approx
0.2521901352777009.
}
\end{equation}

The exact quartic Jacobian baseline is
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
J_f^{(4)}
=
\begin{pmatrix}
10.095240102467715 & -4.991665534361308 & 0.41377801570768584\\
-4.991665534361308 & 22.64650843997611 & -1.2413340471230576\\
0.41377801570768584 & -1.2413340471230576 & 3.6033950146440785
\end{pmatrix}.
}
\end{equation}

Its smallest eigenvalue is
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
\lambda_*:=\lambda_{\min}(J_f^{(4)})\approx 3.5212959,
}
\end{equation}
its operator norm is
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
\|J_f^{(4)}\|_2\approx 24.4716494789482,
}
\end{equation}
and
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
\|\delta_f\|
=
\sqrt{(0.0096)^2+(-0.0288)^2+(0.0096)^2}
\approx
0.03183959798741184.
}
\end{equation}

Because the quartic residual is cubic-homogeneous,
\[
J_f^{(4)}\delta_f=3G_f^{(4)},
\]
so the exact quartic-only Newton correction is
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
\Delta_f^{(4)}
=
-\bigl(J_f^{(4)}\bigr)^{-1}G_f^{(4)}
=
-\frac13\delta_f
=
(-0.0032,\ 0.0096,\ -0.0032).
}
\end{equation}
Hence
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
\|\Delta_f^{(4)}\|
=
\frac13\|\delta_f\|
\approx
0.01061319932913728.
}
\end{equation}

\section{2. Exact decomposition of the unknown local structure}

Define the full Jacobian and full corrected residual vector:
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
J_f:=DG(\delta_f),
\qquad
G_f:=G(\delta_f).
}
\end{equation}

Now define the remainders
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
R_f:=J_f-J_f^{(4)},
\qquad
E_f:=G_f-G_f^{(4)}.
}
\end{equation}

Then
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
J_f=J_f^{(4)}+R_f,
\qquad
G_f=G_f^{(4)}+E_f.
}
\end{equation}

The local uncertainty is therefore completely encoded by the pair
\[
(R_f,E_f).
\]

\section{3. Strong nonquartic forcing already visible at vector level}

Because \(\|G_f\|\) and \(\|G_f^{(4)}\|\) are known, the reverse triangle inequality gives
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
\bigl|\|G_f^{(4)}\|-\|G_f\|\bigr|
\le
\|E_f\|
\le
\|G_f^{(4)}\|+\|G_f\|.
}
\end{equation}

Substituting the numerical values:
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
0.21427160981790663
\le
\|E_f\|
\le
0.2901086607374952.
}
\end{equation}

Dividing by \(\|G_f^{(4)}\|\approx 0.2521901352777009\), we obtain
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
0.8496431059127669
\le
\frac{\|E_f\|}{\|G_f^{(4)}\|}
\le
1.1503568940872333.
}
\end{equation}

\begin{theorem}[Vector-level no-go for strong quartic sufficiency]
The nonquartic residual vector \(E_f\) is necessarily order-one relative to the quartic baseline:
\[
\frac{\|E_f\|}{\|G_f^{(4)}\|}
\ge
0.8496431059127669.
\]
Therefore the strong form of quartic-local sufficiency is already excluded at vector level.
\end{theorem}

\begin{proof}
Immediate from the reverse triangle inequality.
\end{proof}

\paragraph{Meaning.}
Even if the Jacobian remainder \(R_f\) turns out to be small, the vector remainder \(E_f\) is not.
So quartic control can at best survive in the weak Jacobian-dominance sense, not in the strong full-residual sense.

\section{4. Quantitative invertibility criterion for the full Jacobian}

If
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
\|R_f\|_2<\lambda_*\approx 3.5212959,
}
\end{equation}
then the full Jacobian
\[
J_f=J_f^{(4)}+R_f
\]
is invertible, because
\[
J_f
=
J_f^{(4)}
\Bigl(I+(J_f^{(4)})^{-1}R_f\Bigr),
\]
and
\[
\|(J_f^{(4)})^{-1}R_f\|_2
\le
\frac{\|R_f\|_2}{\lambda_*}
<1.
\]

Moreover,
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
\|J_f^{-1}\|_2
\le
\frac{1}{\lambda_*-\|R_f\|_2}.
}
\end{equation}

So the hard numerical threshold is
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
\|R_f\|_2<3.5212959
\quad\Longrightarrow\quad
J_f\ \text{certainly invertible.}
}
\end{equation}

\section{5. Exact Newton-step remainder identity}

Define the full Newton correction
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
\Delta_f:=-J_f^{-1}G_f.
}
\end{equation}

Using
\[
J_f=J_f^{(4)}+R_f,
\qquad
G_f=G_f^{(4)}+E_f,
\qquad
J_f^{(4)}\Delta_f^{(4)}=-G_f^{(4)},
\]
one gets the exact identity
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
\Delta_f-\Delta_f^{(4)}
=
-
J_f^{-1}\bigl(R_f\Delta_f^{(4)}+E_f\bigr).
}
\label{eq:newton_diff_exact}
\end{equation}

So the deviation from the quartic Newton predictor is driven jointly by the matrix remainder \(R_f\) and vector remainder \(E_f\).

\section{6. Upper bound on the Newton mismatch}

If \(\|R_f\|_2<\lambda_*\), then from \eqref{eq:newton_diff_exact},
\begin{equation}
\|\Delta_f-\Delta_f^{(4)}\|
\le
\|J_f^{-1}\|_2
\bigl(
\|R_f\|_2\,\|\Delta_f^{(4)}\|
+
\|E_f\|
\bigr),
\end{equation}
hence
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
\|\Delta_f+\delta_f/3\|
\le
\frac{
\|E_f\|
+
(\|R_f\|_2/3)\|\delta_f\|
}{
\lambda_*-\|R_f\|_2
}.
}
\end{equation}

This gives the standard perturbative control inequality.

\section{7. Lower bound on the Newton mismatch}

A more useful statement for the present corrected branch is the lower bound.

From \eqref{eq:newton_diff_exact},
\[
R_f\Delta_f^{(4)}+E_f
=
-J_f(\Delta_f-\Delta_f^{(4)}),
\]
so
\[
\|R_f\Delta_f^{(4)}+E_f\|
\le
\|J_f\|_2\,\|\Delta_f-\Delta_f^{(4)}\|.
\]
Therefore
\begin{equation}
\|\Delta_f-\Delta_f^{(4)}\|
\ge
\frac{
\|E_f\|-\|R_f\|_2\,\|\Delta_f^{(4)}\|
}{
\|J_f\|_2
}.
\end{equation}

Using
\[
\|J_f\|_2\le \|J_f^{(4)}\|_2+\|R_f\|_2,
\]
we obtain
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
\|\Delta_f+\delta_f/3\|
\ge
\frac{
\|E_f\|-(\|R_f\|_2/3)\|\delta_f\|
}{
\|J_f^{(4)}\|_2+\|R_f\|_2
},
}
\label{eq:newton_lower_bound}
\end{equation}
whenever the numerator is positive.

Dividing by \(\|\delta_f\|\), this yields the normalized lower bound
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
\mathfrak D_{\rm Newt}
:=
\frac{\|\Delta_f+\delta_f/3\|}{\|\delta_f\|}
\ge
\frac{
\|E_f\|/\|\delta_f\|-\|R_f\|_2/3
}{
\|J_f^{(4)}\|_2+\|R_f\|_2
}.
}
\label{eq:newton_lower_norm}
\end{equation}

\section{8. Concrete numerical consequences}

Because
\[
\|E_f\|\ge 0.21427160981790663,
\qquad
\|\delta_f\|\approx 0.03183959798741184,
\]
we have
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
\frac{\|E_f\|}{\|\delta_f\|}
\ge
6.729308620179073.
}
\end{equation}

Therefore \eqref{eq:newton_lower_norm} becomes
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
\mathfrak D_{\rm Newt}
\ge
\frac{
6.729308620179073-\|R_f\|_2/3
}{
24.4716494789482+\|R_f\|_2
}.
}
\end{equation}

Now evaluate this in the strong and moderate Jacobian-dominance regimes.

\paragraph{Strong quartic Jacobian dominance.}
If
\[
\boxed{
\|R_f\|_2\le 0.2\,\lambda_*
=
0.70425918,
}
\]
then
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
\mathfrak D_{\rm Newt}\ge 0.2579834549109606.
}
\end{equation}

\paragraph{Very strong quartic Jacobian dominance.}
If
\[
\boxed{
\|R_f\|_2\le 0.1\,\lambda_*
=
0.35212959,
}
\]
then
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
\mathfrak D_{\rm Newt}\ge 0.2663713855973499.
}
\end{equation}

\paragraph{Moderate quartic Jacobian dominance.}
If
\[
\boxed{
\|R_f\|_2\le 0.5\,\lambda_*
=
1.76064795,
}
\]
then
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
\mathfrak D_{\rm Newt}\ge 0.23417080882818148.
}
\end{equation}

\begin{theorem}[No-go for strong Newton-level quartic sufficiency]
Even if the Jacobian remainder \(R_f\) lies in the strong quartic-dominance regime
\[
\|R_f\|_2\le 0.2\,\lambda_*,
\]
the normalized Newton mismatch still obeys
\[
\mathfrak D_{\rm Newt}\ge 0.2579834549109606.
\]
Therefore the corrected branch cannot be strongly quartic-local even in the optimistic small-\(R_f\) regime.
\end{theorem}

\begin{proof}
Substitute \(\|R_f\|_2\le 0.70425918\) into \eqref{eq:newton_lower_norm}.
\end{proof}

\section{9. Honest interpretation}

The quartic baseline can still dominate the \emph{Jacobian} while failing to dominate the \emph{residual vector}.
That is now the only viable weak notion of quartic-local sufficiency.

So after extracting \(J_f\), the correct question is no longer
\[
\text{``Is the corrected branch almost purely quartic?''}
\]
because the answer is already no.

The correct question is instead
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
\text{What matrix type does }R_f=J_f-J_f^{(4)}\text{ have?}
}
\end{equation}

Namely:
\begin{itemize}
\item small,
\item approximately diagonal,
\item symmetric but full,
\item substantially antisymmetric,
\item or coarse-basis-failing in the adapted tangent/transverse split.
\end{itemize}

\section{Final summary}

The corrected residual vector already forces a large nonquartic contribution:
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
0.21427160981790663
\le
\|E_f\|
\le
0.2901086607374952,
}
\end{equation}
hence
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
\frac{\|E_f\|}{\|G_f^{(4)}\|}
\ge
0.8496431059127669.
}
\end{equation}

So strong quartic-local sufficiency is already excluded at vector level.

If the Jacobian remainder satisfies
\[
\|R_f\|_2<3.5212959,
\]
then \(J_f\) is certainly invertible. But even in the strong Jacobian-dominance regime
\[
\|R_f\|_2\le 0.70425918,
\]
the normalized Newton mismatch still satisfies
\[
\boxed{
\mathfrak D_{\rm Newt}\ge 0.2579834549109606.
}
\]

Therefore the next exact task is sharply fixed:
\[
\boxed{
\text{extract }J_f,\ \text{compute }R_f=J_f-J_f^{(4)},\ \text{and classify the matrix type of }R_f.
}
\]

\section{Final summary}

\begin{equation}
\boxed{
\text{Current status: strong quartic-local sufficiency is already ruled out before }J_f\text{ is extracted.}
}
\end{equation}

The corrected branch is frozen at
\[
\delta_f=(0.0096,-0.0288,0.0096),
\qquad
\|G(\delta_f)\|\approx 0.03791852545979427,
\]
while the exact quartic baseline satisfies
\[
\|G_f^{(4)}\|\approx 0.2521901352777009.
\]

Hence the nonquartic vector remainder
\[
E_f:=G_f-G_f^{(4)}
\]
already obeys
\[
\boxed{
0.21427160981790663
\le
\|E_f\|
\le
0.2901086607374952,
}
\]
so in relative size
\[
\boxed{
\frac{\|E_f\|}{\|G_f^{(4)}\|}
\ge
0.8496431059127669.
}
\]

Therefore quartic-local sufficiency fails in the strong vector-level sense.

The only remaining local unknown is the Jacobian remainder
\[
R_f:=J_f-J_f^{(4)}.
\]
If
\[
\|R_f\|_2<3.5212959,
\]
then \(J_f\) is automatically invertible. But even in the strong Jacobian-dominance regime
\[
\|R_f\|_2\le 0.70425918,
\]
the normalized Newton mismatch must still satisfy
\[
\boxed{
\mathfrak D_{\rm Newt}\ge 0.2579834549109606.
}
\]

So after extracting \(J_f\), the correct next question is not whether the corrected branch is almost purely quartic.
That is already false.
The correct next question is:

\[
\boxed{
\text{what matrix type does }R_f=J_f-J_f^{(4)}\text{ have?}
}
\]

\begin{equation}
\boxed{
\text{Goal C: extract }J_f\text{ and compute }R_f=J_f-J_f^{(4)}.
}
\end{equation}
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
\text{Goal M: classify }R_f\text{ as small / diagonal / symmetric / skew / coarse-basis-failing.}
}
\end{equation}
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
\text{Goal S: determine whether the next reduced refinement is a quadratic matrix layer or a sign-resolved suborbit upgrade.}
}
\end{equation}

\section{Exact Classification Theorem for the Jacobian Remainder}

\section{Short explanation}

At the corrected residual stage, the next finite object is
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
J_f:=DG(\delta_f),
\qquad
\delta_f=(0.0096,-0.0288,0.0096).
}
\end{equation}

The exact quartic baseline is already known:
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
J_f^{(4)}
=
\begin{pmatrix}
10.095240102467715 & -4.991665534361308 & 0.41377801570768584\\
-4.991665534361308 & 22.64650843997611 & -1.2413340471230576\\
0.41377801570768584 & -1.2413340471230576 & 3.6033950146440785
\end{pmatrix},
}
\end{equation}
with
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
\lambda_*:=\lambda_{\min}(J_f^{(4)})\approx 3.5212959,
\qquad
\|J_f^{(4)}\|_F\approx 26.09636693908683.
}
\end{equation}

The only remaining local matrix uncertainty is therefore
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
R_f:=J_f-J_f^{(4)}.
}
\end{equation}

The purpose of this section is to classify \(R_f\) exactly by orthogonal projection, so that one line of norm computations determines the next correct refinement.

\section{1. Strong quartic-local sufficiency is already excluded}

Let
\[
G_f:=G(\delta_f),
\qquad
G_f^{(4)}:=G^{(4)}(\delta_f).
\]
The corrected branch data give
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
\|G_f\|\approx 0.03791852545979427,
\qquad
\|G_f^{(4)}\|\approx 0.2521901352777009.
}
\end{equation}

Hence for
\[
E_f:=G_f-G_f^{(4)},
\]
the reverse triangle inequality gives
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
0.21427160981790663
\le
\|E_f\|
\le
0.2901086607374952,
}
\end{equation}
so in relative size
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
\frac{\|E_f\|}{\|G_f^{(4)}\|}
\ge
0.8496431059127669.
}
\end{equation}

Therefore the strong form of quartic-local sufficiency is already ruled out at vector level.
The only remaining question is the \emph{matrix type} of \(R_f\).

\section{2. First scalar size parameter}

Define the quartic-relative Jacobian size
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
\alpha_f
:=
\frac{\|R_f\|_2}{\lambda_*}.
}
\end{equation}

This is the unique hard perturbative parameter.

\begin{theorem}[Invertibility threshold]
If
\[
\alpha_f<1,
\]
then \(J_f\) is automatically invertible.
Moreover,
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
\|J_f^{-1}\|_2
\le
\frac{1}{\lambda_*(1-\alpha_f)}.
}
\end{equation}
\end{theorem}

\begin{proof}
Write
\[
J_f
=
J_f^{(4)}\Bigl(I+(J_f^{(4)})^{-1}R_f\Bigr).
\]
Since
\[
\|(J_f^{(4)})^{-1}R_f\|_2
\le
\frac{\|R_f\|_2}{\lambda_*}
=
\alpha_f<1,
\]
the Neumann series converges.
\end{proof}

\paragraph{Operational values.}
The exact numerical thresholds are
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
\alpha_f\le 0.1
\iff
\|R_f\|_2\le 0.35212959,
}
\end{equation}
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
\alpha_f\le 0.2
\iff
\|R_f\|_2\le 0.70425918,
}
\end{equation}
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
\alpha_f\le 0.5
\iff
\|R_f\|_2\le 1.76064795.
}
\end{equation}

\section{3. Exact orthogonal decomposition in the coarse basis}

Write
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
R_f=D_f+C_f+A_f,
}
\end{equation}
where
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
D_f:=\operatorname{diag}(R_f),
}
\end{equation}
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
C_f:=\frac12(R_f+R_f^T)-D_f,
}
\end{equation}
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
A_f:=\frac12(R_f-R_f^T).
}
\end{equation}

Then:
\begin{itemize}
\item \(D_f\) is diagonal,
\item \(C_f\) is symmetric and off-diagonal,
\item \(A_f\) is antisymmetric.
\end{itemize}

These three subspaces are Frobenius-orthogonal. Therefore:
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
\|R_f\|_F^2
=
\|D_f\|_F^2+\|C_f\|_F^2+\|A_f\|_F^2.
}
\label{eq:orth_decomp}
\end{equation}

\begin{theorem}[Best-approximation theorem]
\(D_f\) is the unique Frobenius-best diagonal approximation to \(R_f\), and
\[
D_f+C_f=\frac12(R_f+R_f^T)
\]
is the unique Frobenius-best symmetric approximation to \(R_f\).
\end{theorem}

\begin{proof}
Both statements are orthogonal projection theorems onto closed linear subspaces of \(M_3(\mathbb R)\) under the Frobenius inner product.
\end{proof}

\section{4. Exact relative defects}

Define the diagonal-fit defect
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
\varepsilon_{\rm diag}
:=
\frac{\|R_f-D_f\|_F}{\|R_f\|_F}
=
\frac{\|C_f+A_f\|_F}{\|R_f\|_F}
=
\sqrt{\frac{\|C_f\|_F^2+\|A_f\|_F^2}{\|R_f\|_F^2}}.
}
\end{equation}

Define the symmetric-fit defect
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
\varepsilon_{\rm sym}
:=
\frac{\|R_f-(D_f+C_f)\|_F}{\|R_f\|_F}
=
\frac{\|A_f\|_F}{\|R_f\|_F}.
}
\end{equation}

Define the skew fraction
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
\varepsilon_{\rm skew}
:=
\frac{\|A_f\|_F}{\|R_f\|_F}
=
\varepsilon_{\rm sym}.
}
\end{equation}

Define the pure off-diagonal symmetric fraction
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
\varepsilon_{\rm off}
:=
\frac{\|C_f\|_F}{\|R_f\|_F}.
}
\end{equation}

By \eqref{eq:orth_decomp},
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
1
=
\frac{\|D_f\|_F^2}{\|R_f\|_F^2}
+
\varepsilon_{\rm off}^2
+
\varepsilon_{\rm skew}^2.
}
\end{equation}

So the remainder classification is reduced to two independent relative fractions:
\[
\varepsilon_{\rm off},\qquad \varepsilon_{\rm skew}.
\]

\section{5. Exact decision theorem in the coarse basis}

Let \(\tau\in(0,1)\) be the desired structural tolerance.

\begin{theorem}[Coarse-basis decision theorem]
For the extracted Jacobian remainder \(R_f\):

\begin{enumerate}
\item If
\[
\varepsilon_{\rm diag}\le \tau,
\]
then the best diagonal coarse correction \(D_f\) reproduces \(R_f\) with relative Frobenius error at most \(\tau\). In this case an orbitwise quadratic normalization layer is sufficient to relative accuracy \(\tau\).

\item If
\[
\varepsilon_{\rm diag}>\tau
\qquad\text{but}\qquad
\varepsilon_{\rm sym}\le \tau,
\]
then no diagonal correction is sufficient at relative accuracy \(\tau\), but a full symmetric coarse \(3\times 3\) quadratic matrix is sufficient.

\item If
\[
\varepsilon_{\rm sym}>\tau,
\]
then even the best symmetric coarse correction fails at relative accuracy \(\tau\). In this case the local reduced residual map is genuinely non-gradient at order \(\tau\), and one must keep the projected residual system itself as primary.
\end{enumerate}
\end{theorem}

\begin{proof}
Immediate from the best-approximation theorem and the exact defect formulas.
\end{proof}

\section{6. Adapted-basis decomposition}

Now move to the corrected local orthonormal basis
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
t=\frac{1}{\sqrt{11}}(1,-3,1),
\qquad
n_1=\frac{1}{\sqrt2}(1,0,-1),
\qquad
n_2=\frac{1}{\sqrt{22}}(3,2,3),
}
\end{equation}
and let
\[
B=(t\,|\,n_1\,|\,n_2)\in O(3).
\]

Define
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
\widetilde R_f:=B^T R_f B.
}
\end{equation}

Write
\begin{equation}
\widetilde R_f
=
\begin{pmatrix}
r_{tt} & u^T\\
v & P
\end{pmatrix},
\qquad
u,v\in\mathbb R^2,
\qquad
P\in M_2(\mathbb R).
\end{equation}

Because orthogonal changes of basis preserve the Frobenius norm,
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
\|R_f\|_F^2
=
|r_{tt}|^2+\|u\|^2+\|v\|^2+\|P\|_F^2.
}
\end{equation}

Define the tangent-transverse mixing fraction
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
\varepsilon_{\rm mix}
:=
\frac{\sqrt{\|u\|^2+\|v\|^2}}{\|R_f\|_F},
}
\end{equation}
and the transverse-block fraction
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
\varepsilon_{\perp}
:=
\frac{\|P\|_F}{\|R_f\|_F}.
}
\end{equation}

Then
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
1
=
\frac{|r_{tt}|^2}{\|R_f\|_F^2}
+
\varepsilon_{\rm mix}^2
+
\varepsilon_{\perp}^2.
}
\end{equation}

\section{7. Exact suborbit trigger theorem}

\begin{theorem}[Adapted-basis suborbit trigger]
Fix a structural tolerance \(\tau\in(0,1)\).

\begin{enumerate}
\item If
\[
\varepsilon_{\rm mix}\le \tau,
\]
then the corrected tangent direction \(t\) is linearly decoupled from the two transverse directions to relative accuracy \(\tau\).

\item If
\[
\varepsilon_{\rm mix}>\tau,
\]
then the corrected branch is not cleanly one-dimensional at Jacobian level.
In this case the coarse \((A,E,O)\) branch picture is already failing locally, and sign-resolved suborbit refinement becomes the natural next upgrade.

\item Even if \(\varepsilon_{\rm mix}\le\tau\), a large \(\varepsilon_{\perp}\) means that the transverse sector has substantial internal residual structure that cannot be ignored in the next reduced model.
\end{enumerate}
\end{theorem}

\section{8. Operational one-line classification}

Choose a single tolerance \(\tau_{\rm cert}\), for example
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
\tau_{\rm cert}=0.2.
}
\end{equation}

Then the local decision is immediate from one line of numbers:
\[
\alpha_f,\qquad
\varepsilon_{\rm diag},\qquad
\varepsilon_{\rm sym},\qquad
\varepsilon_{\rm mix}.
\]

The exact operational rule is:

\begin{equation}
\boxed{
\begin{array}{lll}
\alpha_f\ge 1 &\Longrightarrow& \text{quartic baseline no longer controls invertibility;}\\[1mm]
\alpha_f<1,\ \varepsilon_{\rm diag}\le \tau_{\rm cert}
&\Longrightarrow&
\text{diagonal coarse quadratic layer;}\\[1mm]
\alpha_f<1,\ \varepsilon_{\rm diag}>\tau_{\rm cert},\ \varepsilon_{\rm sym}\le \tau_{\rm cert},\ \varepsilon_{\rm mix}\le \tau_{\rm cert}
&\Longrightarrow&
\text{full symmetric coarse }3\times 3\text{ layer;}\\[1mm]
\alpha_f<1,\ \varepsilon_{\rm sym}> \tau_{\rm cert}
&\Longrightarrow&
\text{locally non-gradient residual map;}\\[1mm]
\alpha_f<1,\ \varepsilon_{\rm mix}> \tau_{\rm cert}
&\Longrightarrow&
\text{coarse branch basis fails locally; move to sign-resolved suborbits.}
\end{array}
}
\end{equation}

\section{9. Honest interpretation}

Because the vector-level theorem already gives
\[
\frac{\|E_f\|}{\|G_f^{(4)}\|}\ge 0.8496431059127669,
\]
the branch
\[
\text{``quartic-only local sufficiency''}
\]
is no longer logically available.

So after extracting \(J_f\), the only honest remaining branches are:

\begin{enumerate}
\item quartic Jacobian dominance plus diagonal coarse correction;
\item quartic Jacobian dominance plus full symmetric coarse correction;
\item locally non-gradient residual map;
\item coarse basis failure and suborbit refinement.
\end{enumerate}

\section{Final summary}

The next local frontier is completely finite.

Once \(J_f\) is extracted, define
\[
R_f:=J_f-J_f^{(4)},
\]
then compute
\[
\alpha_f=\frac{\|R_f\|_2}{\lambda_*},
\qquad
\varepsilon_{\rm diag},
\qquad
\varepsilon_{\rm sym},
\qquad
\varepsilon_{\rm mix}.
\]

These four numbers determine the next mathematically correct move.

Thus the next exact task is:
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
\text{extract }J_f,\ \text{form }R_f,\ \text{compute }
(\alpha_f,\varepsilon_{\rm diag},\varepsilon_{\rm sym},\varepsilon_{\rm mix}),
\ \text{and branch accordingly.}
}
\end{equation}

\section{Final summary}

\begin{equation}
\boxed{
\text{Current status: after }J_f\text{ is extracted, the next branch is determined by an exact norm classification theorem for }R_f=J_f-J_f^{(4)}.
}
\end{equation}

The corrected residual stage is frozen at
\[
\delta_f=(0.0096,-0.0288,0.0096),
\qquad
\|G(\delta_f)\|\approx 0.03791852545979427.
\]

The exact quartic baseline is
\[
J_f^{(4)}
=
\begin{pmatrix}
10.095240102467715 & -4.991665534361308 & 0.41377801570768584\\
-4.991665534361308 & 22.64650843997611 & -1.2413340471230576\\
0.41377801570768584 & -1.2413340471230576 & 3.6033950146440785
\end{pmatrix},
\]
with
\[
\lambda_*=\lambda_{\min}(J_f^{(4)})\approx 3.5212959.
\]

Strong quartic-local sufficiency is already excluded at vector level because
\[
\frac{\|G_f-G_f^{(4)}\|}{\|G_f^{(4)}\|}
\ge
0.8496431059127669.
\]

Therefore the only remaining local unknown is the matrix type of
\[
R_f:=J_f-J_f^{(4)}.
\]

The exact one-line classification parameters are
\[
\alpha_f=\frac{\|R_f\|_2}{\lambda_*},
\qquad
\varepsilon_{\rm diag}
=
\frac{\|R_f-\operatorname{diag}(R_f)\|_F}{\|R_f\|_F},
\]
\[
\varepsilon_{\rm sym}
=
\frac{\left\|R_f-\frac12(R_f+R_f^T)\right\|_F}{\|R_f\|_F},
\qquad
\varepsilon_{\rm mix}
=
\frac{\sqrt{\|u\|^2+\|v\|^2}}{\|R_f\|_F}
\quad
\text{in the adapted basis }(t,n_1,n_2).
\]

With a chosen certificate tolerance \(\tau_{\rm cert}\), for example \(\tau_{\rm cert}=0.2\), the exact decision is:

\[
\alpha_f<1,\ \varepsilon_{\rm diag}\le\tau_{\rm cert}
\;\Longrightarrow\;
\text{diagonal coarse quadratic layer;}
\]
\[
\alpha_f<1,\ \varepsilon_{\rm diag}>\tau_{\rm cert},\ \varepsilon_{\rm sym}\le\tau_{\rm cert},\ \varepsilon_{\rm mix}\le\tau_{\rm cert}
\;\Longrightarrow\;
\text{full symmetric coarse }3\times3\text{ layer;}
\]
\[
\alpha_f<1,\ \varepsilon_{\rm sym}>\tau_{\rm cert}
\;\Longrightarrow\;
\text{locally non-gradient residual map;}
\]
\[
\alpha_f<1,\ \varepsilon_{\rm mix}>\tau_{\rm cert}
\;\Longrightarrow\;
\text{coarse basis failure and sign-resolved suborbit refinement.}
\]

\begin{equation}
\boxed{
\text{Goal C: extract }J_f\text{ and form }R_f=J_f-J_f^{(4)}.
}
\end{equation}
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
\text{Goal M: compute }(\alpha_f,\varepsilon_{\rm diag},\varepsilon_{\rm sym},\varepsilon_{\rm mix}).
}
\end{equation}
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
\text{Goal S: choose uniquely between diagonal coarse correction, full symmetric coarse correction, non-gradient residual dynamics, and suborbit refinement.}
}
\end{equation}
